Custom analysis software ; the ready - to - run code is available for download on github : https : / / github . com / kganzinger / Analy sis - Software - for - in - situ - hybridization - data - in - single - cells / . The code is actively maintained , so any bugs should be reported via github or by emailing K . A . G . 3 Methods 3 . 1 Probe Design 1 . Select a 15 – 20 nt section of the rRNA target sequence with the modiﬁcation site in the center and generate its reverse comple - ment as a probe sequence . As with molecular beacons used to detect single mismatches , molecular beacons for MR - FISH should be designed with relatively short probe sequences to maximize the thermodynamic effect of methylation on hybri - dization . Simulate the properties of the probe – target duplex using the DINAMelt [ 15 ] Web Server ( http : / / unafold . rna . albany . edu / ? q ¼ DINAMelt / Hybrid2 ) , using the following parameters : l Temperature range : 0 – 100 (cid:3) C ( these are the default settings ) . l NA ( nucleic acid ) type : DNA ( this is the default setting , see Note 4 ) . l Salt Conditions : [ Na + ] ¼ 1 M and [ Mg 2 + ] ¼ 0 M ( these are the default settings ) . l Initial concentrations : [ A 0 ] and [ B 0 ] ¼ 1 μ M . Molecular beacons which we have successfully used to discriminate between methylated and unmethylated RNA return Δ G values in the range of 22 – 35 kcal mol (cid:1) 1 for the probe – target duplex . Δ G values for molecular beacons that we have used to discriminate single methylations fall within a narrower range ( 22 – 27 . 5 kcal mol (cid:1) 1 ) , but we have not system - atically studied this . 2 . Add 5 – 6 self - complementary nucleotides comprising either all G’s and C’s or containing one A : T pair to each end of the probe sequence ( e . g . , 5 0 - CCGCCX 1 X 2 X 3 X 4 X 5 X 6 X 7 Z X 8 X 9 X 10 X 11 X 12 X 13 X 14 GGCGG - 3 0 , where the underlined section forms the stem of the molecular beacon , Z is complementary to the modiﬁcation site , and X’s are complementary to the bases either side of the modiﬁcation site ) . Avoid stem designs with a G , and especially runs of consecutive G’s , next to the ﬂuores - cent label , because these can quench ﬂuorescence and reduce the signal in FISH ; we normally attach the dye at the 5 0 end of the molecular beacon , but most dyes can be added at either the 5 0 or 3 0 end if required . Imaging rRNA Methylation in Bacteria 95 3 . Use the mfold [ 16 ] webserver ( http : / / unafold . rna . albany . edu / ? q ¼ mfold ) to simulate the secondary structure of the molecular beacon you designed , using a folding temperature of 37 (cid:3) C , [ Na + ] ¼ 1 M and [ Mg 2 + ] ¼ 0 M ( these are the default settings ) . In our experience , the ideal molecular beacon folds into a hairpin loop where the stem is formed by the intended 5 – 6 nucleotides , with a Δ G of 2 . 5 – 7 kcal mol (cid:1) 1 ( and 5 . 5 – - 7 kcal mol (cid:1) 1 for discrimination of single methylations ) and minimal self - complementarity in the loop , but we have not systematically studied this . 4 . Because rRNA is highly structured , it is common to ﬁnd sub - stantial secondary structure in the probe section of the molec - ular beacon . Isolated structures with 2 – 3 base pairs are not normally a problem , but self - complementarity which extends the stem length beyond six base pairs can inhibit hybridization and should be eliminated if possible . Various strategies can achieve this , but typically this will involve removing nucleotides from the original or extended stem . For example , if simulation reveals the structure to be 5 0 - CCGCCX 1 X 2 X 3 X 4 X 5 X 6 X 7 Z X 8 X 9 X 10 X 11 X 12 X 13 X 14 GGCGG - 3 0 due to self - complementarity between X 1 / X 14 and X 2 / X 13 , alternative designs might include 5 0 - CCGX 1 X 2 X 3 X 4 X 5 X 6 X 7 Z X 8 X 9 X 10 X 11 X 12 X 13 X 14 CGG - 3 0 , CCGCX 2 X 3 X 4 X 5 X 6 X 7 Z X 8 X 9 X 10 X 11 X 12 X 13 GCGG - 3 0 or CCGCCX 3 X 4 X 5 X 6 X 7 Z X 8 X 9 X 10 X 11 X 12 X 13 X 14 GGCGG - 3 0 . Repeat step 3 until the stem is formed by the intended 5 – 6 nucleotides , with a Δ G of 2 . 5 – 7 kcal mol (cid:1) 1 ( and 5 . 5 – 7 kcal mol (cid:1) 1 for discrimination of single methyla - tions ) . This can require multiple iterations ( see Note 5 ) . 5 . Estimate the extent of discrimination between methylated and unmethylated targets with the DINAMelt Web Server ( http : / / unafold . rna . albany . edu / ? q ¼ DINAMelt / Hybrid2 ) by simulat - ing melting of your full molecular beacon sequence with the target sequence , using the following parameters : l Temperature range : 0 – 100 (cid:3) C ( these are the default settings ) . l NA ( nucleic acid ) type : DNA ( this is the default setting , see Note 4 ) . l Salt Conditions : [ Na + ] ¼ 1 M and [ Mg 2 + ] ¼ 0 M ( these are the default settings ) . l Initial concentrations : [ A 0 ] and [ B 0 ] ¼ 1 μ M . Repeat this after modifying the target or molecular beacon sequence to have a C : A or G : T mismatch at the methylation site . The smallest difference in simulated melting temperature between the fully matched and mismatched beacon – target duplexes ( labeled AB in the generated plot ) for molecular 96 Kristina A . Ganzinger et al . beacons we have successfully used in MR - FISH is > 15 (cid:3) C . If the difference is much smaller than this in your simulation , we suggest modifying the beacon design , by increasing the stabil - ity of the stem relative to the stability of the loop section , repeating steps 3 – 5 . This can require several iterations . 6 . Helper DNA oligonucleotides that hybridize adjacent to the rRNA targeted by the molecular beacon can be used to increase hybridization efﬁciency if required ( e . g . , in case of low signal in FISH ) [ 17 ] . This is more likely when the methylation site is in a relatively inaccessible region of the ribosome [ 18 , 19 ] . To design these , select the 15 – 20 nt of the rRNA target sequence immediately adjacent to each end of the molecular beacon’s binding site and generate their reverse complement to give a helper probe sequence . Estimate the stability of the duplex made by the helper probes with the DINAMelt Web Server ( http : / / unafold . rna . albany . edu / ? q ¼ DINAMelt / Hybrid2 ) by simulating melting of each probe with their reverse comple - ment , using the following parameters : l Temperature range : 0 – 100 (cid:3) C ( these are the default settings ) . l NA ( nucleic acid ) type : DNA ( this is the default setting , see Note 4 ) . l Salt Conditions : [ Na + ] ¼ 1 M and [ Mg 2 + ] ¼ 0 M ( these are the default settings ) . l Initial concentrations : [ A 0 ] and [ B 0 ] ¼ 1 μ M . Helper oligonucleotides that we have successfully used for MR - FISH return Δ G values of 30 – 40 kcal mol (cid:1) 1 when simulated with these parameters . Lengthen or shorten the DNA sequence if Δ G falls far outside this range . To generate a second pair of helper sequences , select the 15 – 20 nt of the rRNA target sequence immediately adjacent to each end tar - geted by the ﬁrst set of helper probes and simulate melting as above , applying the same selection criterion ( Δ G of 30 – 40 kcal mol (cid:1) 1 ) . 3 . 2 Bacterial Culture Use sterile technique up to the point of ﬁxing the bacteria . For best results use a sterile laminar ﬂow hood or if this is not available work close to a ﬂame and avoid leaving cultures , broth or agar exposed to the laboratory environment for longer than necessary ( see Note 6 ) . 1 . Grow isolated colonies of each strain you plan to use to gener - ate biological replicates . Using a single use sterile plastic loop , streak out a glycerol stock onto an LB agar plate and incubate at 37 (cid:3) C overnight for 12 – 18 h . 2 . Prepare a starter culture . Aliquot 10 mL of sterile LB broth into sterilized glass universal tubes . Prepare three tubes per Imaging rRNA Methylation in Bacteria 97 bacterial strain . Using a single - use sterile plastic loop , pick a well isolated single bacterial colony from the cultured agar plate and dip the loop into one of the 10 mL aliquots of the LB broth . Repeat so you have three starter cultures per strain . Incubate these at 37 (cid:3) C overnight for 12 – 18 h on an orbital shaker at 180 rpm . 3 . Inoculate experimental culture . Aliquot 50 mL of sterile LB broth into sterilized , Pyrex 250 mL conical ﬂasks . Prepare one ﬂask per starter culture . Using a clean , sterile pipette tip , add 0 . 5 mL of your starter culture . Incubate at 37 (cid:3) C until the culture reaches mid - log phase ( see Note 7 ) . 4 . Inactivate ( i . e . , ﬁx ) bacteria with formaldehyde . When the OD 600 is between 0 . 4 and 0 . 8 units , pour 7 . 5 mL of bacterial culture into a clean , sterile 15 mL centrifuge tube . Add 7 . 5 mL of 4 % formaldehyde in PBS and leave the resulting suspension at room temperature for 90 min . 5 . Wash inactivated bacteria . Centrifuge the suspension for 10 min at 4000 g to pellet the inactivated cells . Carefully pour off the supernatant and invert the tube over a piece of clean tissue to remove the residual liquid . Add 10 mL of PBS and gently suspend the pellet by pipetting the contents of the tube up and down . Repeat this twice to wash off the majority of the formaldehyde . 6 . Storage of inactivated bacteria . Add 0 . 5 mL of PBS to the washed pellet of inactivated cells and suspend by gently pipet - ting up and down . Transfer to a clean , sterile 1 . 5 mL micro - centrifuge tube . Add 0 . 5 mL of ethanol . The expected yield of 1 mL at ~ 4 . 5 (cid:4) 10 9 ﬁxed E . coli per mL is sufﬁcient for ~ 25 – 50 MR - FISH experiments . Store at (cid:1) 20 (cid:3) C until required . 3 . 3 In Situ Hybridization 1 . Pellet ~ 1 . 35 (cid:4) 10 8 ﬁxed E . coli by centrifugation , resuspend in hybridization buffer , pellet by centrifugation and then resus - pend in 20 μ L of hybridization buffer , containing 1 μ M methylation - insensitive molecular beacon and 1 μ M methylation - sensitive molecular beacon ( in our case these are typically Alexa Fluor - 647 and Alexa Fluor - 488 labeled respec - tively , but these can be substituted with any pair of dyes spec - trally resolved by your microscope , see Subheading 2 . 1 ) . Incubate at 37 (cid:3) C for 21 h ( see Note 8 ) with agitation at 200 rpm . 2 . Pellet cells by centrifugation , resuspend in 30 μ L wash buffer A , then agitate at 200 rpm for 15 min at 37 (cid:3) C . 3 . Pellet cells by centrifugation , resuspend in 30 μ L wash buffer B , then agitate at 200 rpm for 15 min at 37 (cid:3) C . 4 . Pellet cells by centrifugation and resuspend in 10 μ L imaging buffer . 98 Kristina A . Ganzinger et al . 5 . Attach a Frame - Seal slide chamber to a coverslip , add 5 μ L of the cell suspension and a drop of mounting medium to the coverslip . Seal with another plasma - cleaned coverslip and allow to set for at least 30 min at room temperature before imaging ( see Note 9 ) . 3 . 4 Fluorescence Microscopy 1 . Collect ﬂuorescent images for each color , taking care to mini - mize bleed - through between channels by selecting appropriate ﬁlters and acquiring the channels sequentially if required . 2 . Adapt the exposure time to the dynamic range of your camera and sample . If you are imaging a range of samples , start , if possible , with the samples for which you expect your maximal signal . Pick an exposure time ( and camera gain ) for which the average intensity of your signal is about half the dynamic range of your camera . Use 16 - bit imaging if possible , as this will increase the dynamic range , allowing you to resolve smaller intensity differences between bacteria . 3 . Collect sufﬁcient images to yield > 450 cells per sample for analysis after thresholding . In our experience , for samples prepared as in Subheading 3 . 3 , this requires imaging an area of ~ 0 . 7 mm 2 , or approximately 50 ﬁelds of view of area 82 (cid:4) 164 μ m . 3 . 5 Image Analysis 3 . 5 . 1 Data Prerequisites 1 . Data need to be in . tif format with separate , equally sized images for the different colors . ImageJ [ 20 ] can be used to convert images to . tif format . Set the exact ﬁle extension ( . tif , . tiff , . stk ) in Graphical User Interface 1 ( GUI - 1 ) ( see Subhead - ing 3 . 5 , step 7 and Fig . 2a ) . To get started , all the data from our previously published work are available on ﬁgshare and can be used as test data for the code [ 21 ] . 2 . Pairs or triplets of ﬁles need to be identiﬁed by their ﬁle name , which must start with an Arabic number ( index ) , followed by the letter “B” or “R” ( or “Y” for three - color data ) to indicate the color channel ( e . g . , 1B , 1R or 23R , 23B , 23Y ) ( see Note 10 for instructions on how to change this nomenclature if desired ) . 3 . Batch processing of a series of datasets is implemented ; datasets need to be organized in directories ( one directory per dataset ) . Since run time linearly increases with dataset size , we recom - mend optimizing the analysis on a single dataset ﬁrst to select the analysis parameters before running larger datasets . 3 . 5 . 2 Running the Software 1 . Copy the main function Single _ cell _ FISH _ analysis . m and the source code ( folder : “source codes” ) into a local directory . 2 . These include parameters that are speciﬁc to a dataset , such as the dataset name and image width . Other parameters that need adaptation are those used to detect bacteria in the ﬂuorescent images , as this requires setting a threshold for detection that needs to be empirically opti - mized . The higher concentration of DEPC is required because Tris deactivates it via chemical reaction . Alternatively , dissolve Tris – HCl in DEPC - treated water ( see Subheading 2 . 1 , item 2 ) . 2 . The formamide concentration can be optimized to enhance signal and / or discrimination of methylation . Because formam - ide destabilizes the molecular beacon stem , rRNA secondary structure , and the probe – target duplex , it is difﬁcult to predict the appropriate concentration for a given set of probes and methylations , so we determine this empirically if needed ( i . e . , when 20 % formamide gives low signal and / or poor discrimina - tion of methylation ) . We found that using an inexpensive , low - magniﬁcation objec - tive ( Plan Fluor (cid:4) 20 air , numerical aperture ( NA ) 0 . 50 , Nikon ) gave similar results as objectives with a higher numerical aper - ture ( CFI Plan Apochromat λ (cid:4) 40 air , NA 0 . 95 and (cid:4) 60 Plan Apo TIRF oil immersion , NA 1 . 45 , both by Nikon ) , with the added advantage of higher throughput due to the larger ﬁeld - of - view obtained at lower magniﬁcations . 4 . There is currently no option for DNA / RNA hybrids . 5 . Remove bases from the loop section with caution , as hairpin loops with very small loops may be too stable to hybridize with the target sequence ; this will be reﬂected in a large Δ G of the hairpin loop structure . In our experience , loop lengths below 12 nucleotides can require stems shorter than six base pairs to hybridize . Shift methylation site from center if needed or shorten the stem . 6 . The protocol given is for culturing Escherichia coli BW25113 . If culturing different strains or species , refer to any available data as the optimal culturing conditions may require modiﬁca - tions to the procedure given here . If the strain you are using includes an antibiotic resistance gene or is transformed with a plasmid containing an antibiotic resistance gene , you can include appropriate antibiotics at the appropriate concentration in the LB agar and LB broth . 7 . Under optimal conditions , mid - log phase is usually reached in 2 – 3 h . The most convenient method to monitor bacterial growth is to transfer 1 mL of culture to a 1 cm pathlength cuvette using a sterile pipette and measure the absorbance at 600 nm ( OD 600 ) in a spectrophotometer . Mid - log can be deﬁned as when the OD 600 is between 0 . 4 and 0 . 8 units . 8 . The duration of in situ hybridization can be varied . Methyla - tion can be discriminated within 0 . 5 h of hybridization [ 8 ] , but this will vary depending on the hybridization kinetics of the speciﬁc probes used . 9 . Cells which have had insufﬁcient time to rehydrate in imaging buffer have poor morphology when imaged . 10 . The letter code for the color can be changed in function “get _ tiffs” or “get _ tiffs _ threecolours” in lines 10 , 11 , 12 . 11 . We use only the signal from the red channel for bacteria detec - tion , since the red - labeled methylation - insensitive probe is used to label ribosomes irrespective of methylation state . Using only the red channel thus minimized bias due to the expected large variation in ﬂuorescence brightness in the green and yellow channels ( reporting the methylation state ) . How - ever , in different experiments it may be beneﬁcial to ﬁrst detect bacteria in in all channels , and then select objects based on Imaging rRNA Methylation in Bacteria 103 whether they are recorded in any channel ( “OR” criterion ) or all channels [ “AND” criterion ; in principle , this can reduce the number of false positive events ( e . g . , detecting background rather than bacteria ) ] . This is already implemented for two - color data . 12 . To detect bacteria , ﬁrst the 2D Fourier transform of the image is calculated and a low - pass ﬁlter is applied that removes fre - quencies corresponding to structures smaller than 8 μ m . After this , the ﬁltered image is obtained by reverse Fourier transfor - mation , then converted to a binary image ( mask ) using a threshold intensity , set to be the mean single pixel intensity of the ﬁltered red image plus “initial threshold” times this value . This threshold is set by parameter “ initial threshold ” in GUI - 1 ( Fig . 2a and Table 2 ) . Pixels with intensity values below the threshold are set to zero and considered to be background , while pixels with values above the threshold are set to one and potential candidates for bacteria signals . Good values for the “initial threshold” need to be determined empirically by visual inspection of the resulting mask and comparison with the original data , and will depend on the signal - to - noise ratio of the data ( Fig . 3b – d ) . To display this mask during an analysis run , the parameter “ Display detected bacs ” should be set to 1 ( Table 2 ) . The analysis will be paused as long as the image is displayed ; press “space” to continue to the next image of a dataset . 13 . After obtaining an image mask , objects ( bacteria ) are detected in the next step by tracing continuous regions of ones in the binary image ( mask ) . We have previously only used the signal from the red channel for bacteria detection , and apply this mask to both color channels , but it is also possible to create a mask from each channel individually . Segmentation of individual cells is directly dependent on the bacteria density in the sample , so this may require some optimization in the sample prepara - tion step ( see also Subheading 3 . 3 ) . However , we encountered aggregates of bacteria under any conditions we tried . Since these aggregates may consist of bacteria belonging to different strains , it is necessary to include further selection criteria for robust discrimination of single cells and cell aggregates . We have explored selection criteria based on region size , continu - ity , and ellipticity . We expected that single cells should have , on average , a higher ellipticity than aggregates and cell debris , but we found that this criterion did not improve discrimination independently of the threshold ellipticity value used . However , this could potentially aid discrimination for other bacteria , and this criterion can be activated if the parameter “ threshold based on perimeter / area ratio ” is set to 1 ( Table 2 ) . For our data analysis , we selected regions based on their size 104 Kristina A . Ganzinger et al . and continuity : regions were assumed to correspond to single bacteria if the area of the region is within a certain size range by setting a lower and upper threshold ( parameter : “ lower / upper threshold bac size , ” Table 2 ) . It is best to estimate the expected size distribution by manual analysis of the raw data , for example , using ImageJ [ 20 ] . A size threshold of 1 – 10 μ m 2 worked well for E . coli bacteria ( Fig . 3e , f ) . The Fig . 3 Effect of thresholding parameters on image segmentation ( detection of bacteria ) . ( a ) Typical raw data ; heat map goes from blue ( low ) to red ( high ) intensity . ( b – d ) Effect of parameter “initial threshold” on converting the raw data into a binary image in which ROI ( bacteria ) are separated from the background . If the threshold is too low ( b , c ) , multiple bacteria in close proximity show up as a single ROI ( red arrows ) . ( e – f ) Effect of parameters “lower / upper size threshold” on selecting only ROIs corresponding to single bacteria . The range needs to be adjusted to the expected size distribution in order to achieve good discrimination ( cf . also ( g ) ) . If the parameter “Display detected bacs” is set to 1 , these images ( showing selected ROI by tracing them on the mask image generated ) are displayed for each data ﬁle analyzed . ( g ) Distribution of bacteria sizes in ( a ) as determined from the analysis software after parameter optimization ( see image in e ) Imaging rRNA Methylation in Bacteria 105 continuity criterion is set by the parameter “ maximum num of empty px in ROI ” and it corresponds to the number of pixels with value zero contained within the region of interest ( group of pixels of value 1 , Table 2 ) . Since these “empty” regions most likely correspond to the space between bacteria incorrectly merged into one single region , introducing this parameter helps to discriminate between groups of bacteria and individual cells . We required regions to contain fewer than 50 background pixels ( value zero ) , corresponding to a total area of 5 . 15 μ m 2 . Gerst Abstract In eukaryotic cells , a small percentage of mRNA molecules can undergo transfer from one cell to another . mRNA transfer occurs primarily via membrane nanotubes , which are long thin protrusions that are produced by numerous cell types and can connect cells that can be up to hundreds of microns apart . Potentially , mRNAs might also transfer via extracellular vesicles ( EVs ) . Here we describe a method to detect transferred mRNA in cocultures of two different cell types and to distinguish between nanotube - and EVs - mediated transfer . This method uses single molecule ﬂuorescent in situ hybridization ( smFISH ) to provide an accurate and quantitative detection of transferred mRNA molecules and their subcellular localization . Following the guidelines presented here will allow the user to investigate mRNA transfer of most transcripts in any co - culture system . In addition , we present modiﬁcations that improve nanotube preservation during the smFISH procedure . Key words mRNA , Membrane nanotubes , Extracellular vesicles , Exosomes , Single - molecule ﬂuores - cent in situ hybridization , β - Actin , Glutaraldehyde 1 Introduction The gold standard method used to obtain absolute quantitative data on the number of RNA molecules of a speciﬁc transcript in single cells is single - molecule ﬂuorescent in situ hybridization ( smFISH ) [ 1 , 2 ] . This method uses multiple short ﬂuorescent - labeled DNA antisense oligonucleotides which tile along the length of the RNA . This increases the signal - to - noise ( SNR ) ratio and allows for the detection of single molecules and absolute quantita - tive analysis of both transcript numbers and spatial localization within the cell [ 3 ] ( Fig . 1a ) . Recently , we used smFISH to show that mRNA molecules can transfer between mammalian cells through unique cellular exten - sions called membrane nanotubes ( a . k . a . tunneling nanotubes ) [ 4 ] . These nanotubes are long thin protrusions that can be produced by Yaron Shav - Tal ( ed . ) , Imaging Gene Expression : Methods and Protocols , Methods in Molecular Biology , vol . 2038 , https : / / doi . org / 10 . 1007 / 978 - 1 - 4939 - 9674 - 2 _ 8 , © Springer Science + Business Media , LLC , part of Springer Nature 2019 109 many cell types and transfer cargo between cells , such as ions , proteins , miRNA , mRNA , viral particles and whole organelles [ 5 – 8 ] . Nanotubes are structurally distinct from ﬁlopodia [ 9 ] . Nev - ertheless , there are no known nanotube markers and nanotube visualization is achieved primarily by membrane staining or staining for the cytoskeletal ﬁbers ( typically F - actin , although some nano - tubes contain microtubules ) . Since nanotubes are very thin and fragile , it is difﬁcult to image them and their cargo . This is particu - larly true in assays such as smFISH , which requires the ﬁxation of cells , followed by multiple rounds of washing the samples prior to imaging . It was also suggested that mRNA can transfer via extra - cellular vesicles ( EVs ) [ 10 , 11 ] , although some studies suggest that only mRNA fragments ( and not full - length mRNAs ) are present in EVs [ 12 – 14 ] . There are several advantages for using smFISH for detection of transferred mRNA , compared to other methods that detect and quantify RNA ( e . g . RT - qPCR , RNA sequencing or single - cell RNA sequencing ( scRNA - seq ) ) . One major advantage is that smFISH Fig . 1 Different strategies for probe design . ( a ) Left : A schematic depicting 25 – 50 probes that are tiled along the entire length of an mRNA . Right : The image shows FISH staining for β - actin mRNA with Cy5 - labeled probes tiled to the CDS . The cells shown are a wild - type MEF ( right ) cocultured for 3 h with a MBS - MEF ( left ) . ( b ) Left : The probes are designed against a unique tag [ e . g . MS2 - coat binding sequence x24 ( MBS ) or GFP ] genetically integrated into the mRNA sequence . Right : The image of the same cells in ( a ) shows FISH staining for β - actin - MBS mRNA with Cy3 - labeled probes tiled to the MBS tag . It is evident from the FISH signal that the left cell is a donor ( i . e . , MBS - MEF ) , whereas the right cell is an acceptor ( i . e . , wild - type MEF ) . ( c ) Left : The same mRNA is detected by two sets of probes , each labeled with a different color . Right : The image shows merging of the FISH images from ( a ) and ( b ) . Magenta— β - actin CDS Cy5 - labeled probes . Green—MBS Cy3 - labeled probes . Inset : zoom - in on a single transferred mRNA that shows the colocalization of both colors . FISH probe sequences can be found in [ 23 ] . All images are maximum projections of the z - sections 110 Gal Haimovich and Jeffrey E . Gerst allows for spatial resolution of the transcript , which is lost when cells are lysed for RNA extraction , a required step for the other methods . Thus , smFISH makes it possible to determine the spatial organization of both the RNA donor and acceptor cells in the culture , as well as intracellular localization of the mRNA itself . A second advantage to using smFISH is that it does not necessitate cell separation , whereas to measure the level of mRNA transfer by other methods , e . g . RNA - seq , the acceptor cells must be separated completely from donor cells . Due to the low level of mRNA trans - fer , even a minute contamination of donor cells can skew the sequencing results . Third , current scRNA - seq technology has a lower limit of detection of ~ 10 molecules / cell [ 15 ] , whereas smFISH can detect as little as 1 mRNA molecule / cell . It should be noted that all mRNAs that we tested , except for β - actin mRNA , were transferred at levels averaging less than 10 molecules / cell [ 4 ] . Thus , smFISH shows higher sensitivity and is especially useful for detecting low levels of mRNA transfer . Yet there are disadvantages of smFISH compared to RNA - seq . First , only a small number of different transcripts can be detected simultaneously by smFISH , as compared to whole transcriptome analysis afforded by RNA - seq . Second , compared to smFISH , RNA - seq has no difﬁculty in determining transcript variability ( e . g . , splice variants , RNA editing , single - nucleotide polymorph - isms ( SNPs ) ) . However , recent developments in smFISH technol - ogy do allow for the detection of multiple transcripts [ 16 – 18 ] , as well as transcript variations [ 19 – 22 ] , that could be implemented based on the guidelines that are presented here . The method presented here was developed using cocultures of mouse embryonic ﬁbroblasts ( MEFs ) in which donor cells are derived from mice endogenously expressing MS2 - labeled β - actin mRNA [ 23 ] and acceptor cells derived from wild - type ( WT ) mice , as well as using heterologous cocultures of MEFs with human cell lines [ 4 ] . However , this protocol can be used for any combination of cells and for any mRNA ( or long noncoding RNA ) species . In addition , we provide optional modiﬁcations that improve nanotube preservation during the smFISH procedure , although they may occur at the expense of the ability of smFISH to detect transferred mRNA . 2 Materials Prepare all solutions using ultrapure nuclease - free water . Prepare and store all reagents at room temperature , unless otherwise indi - cated . Follow institutional guidelines for working with , and dispos - ing of , hazardous chemicals and biohazard materials . Detection of mRNA Transfer Between Mammalian Cells by smFISH 111 2 . 1 Choosing Cells for Coculture The choice of cells depends on the system being investigated . In order to conﬁdently detect mRNA transfer , the acceptor cells should be completely devoid of the query mRNA prior to cocul - ture . The following guidelines will help in the detection of mRNA transfer in the coculture of choice : 1 . Genetically modify the query mRNA in the donor cells by the addition of a long and unique RNA sequence ( e . g . , we provide the example of MS2x24 labeling , but other long labels such as GFP gene fusions would also work ) ( Fig . 1b ) . Do not use transient transfection of donor cells with the gene encoding the modiﬁed transcript , since the plasmid DNA might transfer as well ( [ 24 ] and our unpublished observations ) . Use only stable cell lines . It should be taken into consideration that addition of such a nucleotide sequence might affect the behav - ior ( e . g . , stability , localization ) of the mRNA and , therefore , its ability to undergo transfer . 2 . Alternatively , the gene encoding the query mRNA can be knocked - out in the acceptor cells . In this case , it is imperative to verify that the knock - out cell - line does not express any fragment of the RNA that can hybridize with the FISH probes being used for detection . 3 . A third possibility is to use a heterologous coculture system of cells that are derived from different species ( e . g . , human vs . mouse ) . In this case , it is important to verify that the gene is either absent in acceptor cells , or that the nucleotide sequence of the mRNA is sufﬁciently different to allow for the use of species - speciﬁc probes ( see also Subheading 2 . 2 ) . 4 . Both cell types should be able to proliferate in the same culture medium and under the same culture conditions . 5 . The researcher should be able to positively distinguish donor from acceptor cells in coculture . In some cases , expression of the query mRNA in donor cells ( i . e . , by examining cytoplasmic mRNA levels and the presence of transcription sites in the nucleus ) is sufﬁcient to distinguish donor from acceptor cells in coculture . However , other means to distinguish the two cell types should be considered ( e . g . , cell morphology , the expres - sion pattern of a ﬂuorescent protein , performing co - FISH with probes for another unique RNA , or by staining one of the cell types with a distinct dye ) ( Fig . 1b , c ) . 2 . 2 Design and Labeling of Oligonucleotide Probes Probes are 18 – 22 mer DNA oligonucleotides that are ﬂuorescently labeled with a ﬂuorescent organic dye at one or both ends . We recommend using the Stellaris probe design web tool ( https : / / www . biosearchtech . com / support / tools / design - software / stellaris - probe - designer ) and to order the Stellaris RNA FISH probes from Biosearch Technologies . The probes arrive in powder 112 Gal Haimovich and Jeffrey E . Gerst form and should be dissolved in nuclease - free water to a concentra - tion of 25 mM . The amount is sufﬁcient for 200 samples , when using the default concentration of 250 nM . For more details and alternative labeling methods , see [ 3 ] . The researcher should consider the following guidelines : 1 . Good detection requires a high signal - to - noise ratio . Typically , a minimal set of 25 probes is required , but more is better . Therefore , the mRNA should be long enough to allow for the creation of at least 25 unique probes . 2 . When using a heterologous system , the 3 0 - UTR and 5 0 - UTR are probably the most divergent sequences . Thus , species - speciﬁc antisense probes could be designed for these regions . It should be noted that a single nucleotide variance within the 20 nt probe is insufﬁcient for speciﬁcity and specialized FISH protocols should be utilized [ 19 – 21 ] . The user should also take into consideration that some transcripts have multiple isoforms that are different in 5 0 - UTR and 3 0 - UTR sequences and / or length due to alternative splicing , alternative transcription start sites , and alternative cleavage and polyadenylation . Thus , the probes might not identify all isoforms . 3 . The expression level of the mRNA in the donor cell could affect the efﬁciency of mRNA transfer [ 4 ] . High expression could lead to increased transfer , but this might not be the case for every mRNA . Moreover , since mRNA transfer rate is ~ 1 – 5 % [ 4 ] , transfer of mRNAs that are expressed at less than 50 – 100 copies / cell in donor cells may have a low chance to be detected over background staining . 4 . When probes are designed to tile along the entire length of the mRNA ( Fig . 1a ) , the full - length mRNA may be detected . However , when probes are designed to a partial sequence ( e . g . a speciﬁc tag or gene sequence , see Subheading 2 . 1 , item 1 , Fig . 1b ) , there is a possibility that only this fragment is detected . In order to distinguish between transfer of a full - length mRNA and that of an mRNA fragment , design two sets of probes using two different colors . One set can be complementary to the 5 0 - UTR and the coding sequence ( CDS ) and the other set complementary to the tag ( Fig . 1c ) . Each set should contain at least 25 probes as explained in Subheading 2 . 2 , item 1 . If it is not possible to design two full sets of donor - unique probes , then one unique set ( i . e . , for the tag ) is sufﬁcient . Co - localization of the signal from the two sets indicates it is a full - length mRNA . 2 . 3 Cell Culture on Fibronectin - Coated Coverslips and Tripods 1 . CCD or sCMOS high - resolution digital camera ( e . g . , Flash 4 sCMOS ( Hamamatsu ) ) . 3 Methods 3 . 1 Cell Culture : Coculture with Cells in Direct Contact For efﬁcient mRNA transfer , we recommend seeding the acceptor cells prior to donor cells [ 4 ] . However , seeding both donor and acceptor cells together is possible . It is imperative to have a negative control culture of acceptor cells alone . It is advisable to have a culture of donor cells alone , as well . Important : when plating the cells , aim for a maximum of ~ 80 – 90 % conﬂuence of the single cultures and co - cultures at the time of ﬁxation ( Subheading 3 . 3 , step 2 ) . See Note 5 . Detection of mRNA Transfer Between Mammalian Cells by smFISH 117 1 . Prewarm PBS 1 (cid:3) , culture medium , and trypsin solution . Pre - pare coating solution ( fresh ) . 2 . For each sample , place a coverslip in a well of a 12 - well plate . Rinse ( add solution and remove after a few seconds ) with 1 mL / well 70 % ethanol , then rinse with 1 mL / well PBS 1 (cid:3) . Add 1 mL / well of ﬁbronectin coating solution ( see Note 1 ) and incubate in a cell culture incubator for 10 – 20 min . Rinse once with 1 mL / well PBS (cid:3) 1 . Add 1 mL / well of culture medium . 3 . Verify that the coverslip does not ﬂoat in the medium and seed acceptor cells of choice on the glass coverslips . Swirl the plate ( medium ) slowly a few times to spread the cells evenly . Incu - bate for several hours to overnight in the cell culture incubator . 4 . Seed the donor cells of choice on top of the acceptor cells . Swirl the plate slowly a few times to spread the cells evenly . Incubate for the desired duration of the co - culture . See Note 6 . 3 . 2 Cell Culture : Coculture with Tripods In order to distinguish between contact - dependent mRNA transfer ( e . g . via nanotubes ) or diffusion - mediated mRNA transfer ( e . g . via EVs ) , the donor and acceptor cells are separated from each other to prevent direct contact , but still share medium that allows for the free diffusion of materials between cells ( see Note 7 ) . Preparation of tripods : 1 . Fill ~ half of a 1 . 7 mL tube with parafﬁn wax ﬂakes and place in a heating block at 110 (cid:2) C . Wait until the parafﬁn melts . 2 . Place coverslips in a 12 - well plate . 3 . Dip a glass Pasteur pipette in the melted parafﬁn ( i . e . , while in the heating block ) . Seal the top of the pipette with your gloved ﬁnger . 4 . Very quickly , place drops of melted parafﬁn , 2 – 3 mm high , in a triangular arrangement , at the edges of the coverslips . Note that the parafﬁn solidiﬁes within a few seconds after removing it from the heating block . 5 . Tripods can be kept dry in a 12 - well plate at room temperature indeﬁnitely . 6 . Tripod coverslips are treated and plated with donor cells as described in Subheading 3 . 1 . Donor cells can be up to 70 – 80 % conﬂuence at the time of coculture , but care should be taken that the cells do not reach full conﬂuence by the end of the designated co - culture time . 7 . Acceptor cells are plated on regular coverslips as described in Subheading 3 . 1 . Acceptor cells can be up to 70 – 80 % conﬂu - ence at time of co - culture , but care should be taken so that the 118 Gal Haimovich and Jeffrey E . Gerst cells do not reach full conﬂuence by the end of the designated coculture time ( see Note 5 ) . 8 . When it is time to begin coculture , use the tweezers to pick up a donor cell - seeded tripod , and ﬂip it , cells facing down , over a coverslip pre - seeded with acceptor cells . Both coverslips should be completely covered by the culture medium . When per - formed correctly , the donor tripod will stand on its three legs at a 2 – 3 mm distance above the acceptor cells . 9 . In tripod experiments , always compare to co - culture of cells in contact and acceptor alone culture ( Subheading 3 . 1 ) . 3 . 3 smFISH 1 . General Comments ( a ) To avoid RNase contamination of samples , wear gloves , use barrier tips and avoid working on surfaces where there is regular use of RNases ( e . g . , from plasmid prep kits ) . ( b ) All steps except “hybridization” are performed while the coverslips remain in the well , with 1 mL of solution added per well . ( c ) Pipet liquids onto the walls of the well and not directly onto the cells . ( d ) Do not shake the plates during any of the incubations or washes . ( e ) At any step—do not let the cells to dry , since they will lose their form and might also acquire autoﬂuorescent back - ground signals . ( f ) Optional : to improve preservation of nanotubes , use the pipet aid at the slowest setting and do not use a vacuum aspirator at all . See also Note 2 and Table 1 for more details on nanotube preservation during smFISH and the consequences . 2 . If performing a tripod experiment ( Subheading 3 . 2 ) , remove the donor ( top ) tripod coverslip ﬁrst . 3 . Rinse cells once with PBSM . 4 . Fix cells by incubating them with ﬁxation buffer ( prepared fresh ) for 10 min at room temperature ( RT ) . 5 . Wash with quenching buffer for 10 min at RT . 6 . Wash with PBSM for 10 min at RT . Repeat this step . Samples can be left overnight at 4 (cid:2) C at this point . 7 . Permeabilize cells by incubating with Permeabilization buffer for 10 min at RT . If the Permeabilization buffer contains phalloidin - FITC , cover the plate with aluminum foil from this step onward . 8 . Wash with PBSM for 10 min at RT . Repeat this step . 9 . Incubate with Prehybridization buffer for 30 min at RT . Detection of mRNA Transfer Between Mammalian Cells by smFISH 119 10 . While waiting ( Subheading 3 . 3 , step 9 ) , mix the thawed , pre - made hybridization buffer with the probes , and prepare the hybridization chamber . 11 . Place 45 μ L of hybridization buffer at each intended coverslip position in the hybridization chamber . There is no need to remove large bubbles , but avoid small foam - like bubbles . 12 . With the tweezers , gently lift each coverslip from the well , remove excess liquid by touching the edge on a Kimwipe and place the coverslips with the cells facing down on the hybridiza - tion buffer . 13 . Alternative : to improve preservation of nanotubes , place cover - slips in the hybridization chamber with cells facing up , and add 45 μ L of hybridization buffer on top of the cells ( pipette from the edge of the coverslip , slowly ) . Make sure that the liquid covers the entire surface . 14 . Seal the hybridization chamber with Paraﬁlm , wrap with alu - minum foil , and place in a 37 (cid:2) C incubator for 3 h to overnight . 15 . Prepare a new ( or same ) 12 - well plate with Prehybridization buffer . 16 . Use the tweezers to transfer the coverslips back to the 12 - well plate , cells facing up . 17 . Cover the plate with aluminum foil and incubate in the 37 (cid:2) C incubator for 15 min . 18 . Wash again in Prehybridization buffer , 15 min at 37 (cid:2) C . 19 . Quick rinse with 2 (cid:3) SSC at RT ( two quick rinses ) . 20 . Stain in DAPI stain solution ( prewarmed to RT ) for 60 – 90 s at RT . 21 . Wash for 5 min with 2 (cid:3) SSC . 22 . During this ﬁnal wash step , label the microscope slide ( s ) . 23 . Just before lifting the coverslips , place 20 μ L of ProLong antifade onto the microscope slides , one drop for each cover - slip ( there can be two per slide ) . Remove any air bubbles . See Note 4 . 24 . Use tweezers to lift coverslips , remove excess liquid on Kim - wipes and place cell - side facing down , on the ProLong anti - fade drop . 25 . Let dry at RT in the dark for at least several hours ( for best images , wait > 24 h ) . 26 . ( Optional ) For long - term storage , seal with nail polish around the edges of the coverslip after the ProLong dries . 27 . Slides can be stored at RT for at least 2 weeks ( we have not checked for longer than that ) . Keep at (cid:4) 20 (cid:2) C for long - term storage ( months to years ) . 120 Gal Haimovich and Jeffrey E . Gerst 3 . 4 Imaging 1 . Imaging can be performed on any wide - ﬁeld microscope that is equipped as detailed in Subheading 2 . 5 . See Note 8 . 2 . Images should be taken at the relevant channels with descend - ing color order ( e . g . , Cy5 ( 670 nm , far - red ) , Cy3 ( 570 nm , yellow - orange ) , FITC ( 520 nm , green ) , and DAPI ( 460 nm , blue ) ) . 3 . Exposure time : for FISH ﬂuorophores , it is recommended to use maximum ( 100 % ) power of the light source and a long exposure time of 1 – 3 s for each FISH channel . In order to detect the thin nanotubes by phalloidin - FITC staining it is recommended to use an exposure time that is longer than typically used for cellular F - actin . This means that cellular F - actin will be over - exposed . For DAPI , use a low power setting and a very short exposure time ( e . g . 30 % power , 20 – 40 ms ) . 4 . Z - sections : In order to detect RNA spots throughout the vol - ume of the cell , multiple z - sections should be collected . It is recommended to use 0 . 2 – 0 . 3 μ m steps . Note that nanotubes are above the substratum and sometimes even pass above a neighboring cell ( Fig . 2 ) so it is better to have z sections that go at least 1 – 2 μ m above the cells . When imaging , choose the option to image all z - sections in one channel before switching to the next channel . 5 . Binning : for better resolution , use pixel binning 1 (cid:3) 1 . How - ever , sometimes for the beneﬁt of signal enhancement over resolution , a 2 (cid:3) 2 binning may be used . 3 . 5 FISH Spot Analysis FISH spot analysis is performed by programs that ﬁt the diffraction - limited spots using a 3D Gaussian ﬁtting algorithm . Many labs produce their own software / scripts . We recommend FISH - quant ( https : / / bitbucket . org / muellerﬂorian / ﬁsh _ quant ) [ 25 , 26 ] since it is user - friendly and can perform multiple analyses ( i . e . , mature mRNA and transcription site analysis ) . The images for FISH - quant need to be in TIFF format as a separate ﬁle of multiple z - sections obtained for each channel . If the microscope does not save ﬁles in TIFF format , convert the images by using another program ( e . g . , FIJI [ 27 ] ) . Follow the instructions accompanying FISH - quant to perform the analysis . Here is a brief description of the steps , with special emphasis on the detection of mRNA transfer : 1 . Install MATLAB ( version 2015b or higher ) and then install FISH - quant . 2 . In the main interface of FISH - quant : ( a ) Choose folders ( e . g . , main folder , images folder , outlines folder , results folder ) . Detection of mRNA Transfer Between Mammalian Cells by smFISH 121 ( b ) Insert the experimental parameters : XY pixel size ( based on camera and binning ) , Z pixel size , refractive index of the immersion oil , NA of the objective and excitation and emission wavelengths of the FISH probe ﬂuorophore . ( c ) Under “Tools” ! “outline designer” : draw outlines for cells , nuclei and transcription sites ( TS ) ( hint : use the automatic “detect nucleus” and “TS auto detect” based on the DAPI and TS / FISH staining , respectively ) . ( d ) Upload an outline ﬁle of an image of a donor cell . ( e ) Filter the background ( try different modes and para - meters for best ﬁltering ) . ( f ) Perform predetection according to the accompanying instructions . ( g ) Fit spots using the Gaussian algorithm . ( h ) Use thresholding parameters to achieve the best detection of mRNA spots . The “Spot inspector” button can assist in estimating this analysis . It does not have to be perfect , this is ﬁne - tuned later . ( i ) Save the detection settings . 3 . From “Tools” ! “Batch processing” it is possible to perform analysis of multiple images . Upload the saved settings ﬁle from Subheading 3 . 5 , step 2 . i and the outlines of all the images , and run the batch processing . 4 . After Batch processing is complete , inspect the thresholded spots as listed in the status window of the “Batch processing” graphical user interface ( GUI ) . The number of spots in cells from acceptor alone cultures should be close to zero . If there are false - positive spots in these cells , highlight an outline ﬁle - name of an image from an acceptor cell only culture in the “Outline ﬁles to be processed” box and use the “Spot inspec - tor” from within the “Batch processing” GUI to check the parameters of the detected false positive spots . Change the thresholding parameters to exclude background spots in accep - tor only cells , but with minimal effect on the number of spots in donor cells . See Note 9 . Thresholding is performed automati - cally when changing the parameters and the status window is updated accordingly . Continue to modify the parameters until satisﬁed . 5 . Save “Results for each image [ All spots ] , ” “Summary : mature mRNA” and the detection settings . 6 . Use “Tools” ! “Spot inspector” to eliminate obvious false positives [ 4 ] ( e . g . , areas of high autoﬂuorescence , spots that are due to inaccurate outlining of the nucleus , and spots that are present at the interface of donor - acceptor cells in the coculture ) . 122 Gal Haimovich and Jeffrey E . Gerst 7 . The data from FISH - quant is saved as a “ . txt” ﬁle . The data can be copied and pasted to Excel for further analysis . 8 . If the mRNA transfers , the number of spots in acceptor cells in coculture will be signiﬁcantly higher than in acceptor culture alone , but signiﬁcantly lower than the number of mRNA spots in donor cells . 3 . 6 Image Analysis We use FIJI for other image analysis requirements . 1 . Use the “Straight” tool to measure the length of nanotubes . Nanotubes will be visible by the phalloidin - FITC staining . Nanotubes connect two cells , are above the substratum and typically go in a straight line ( Fig . 2 ) . Fig . 2 Glutaraldehyde ﬁxation increases nanotube stability . Images of MBS - MEF cultures labeled with Cy3 - MBS probes ( I , III ) and phalloidin - FITC ( II , IV ) after ﬁxation with 4 % PFA ( I , II ; Table 1 , Condition # 3 ) or with 3 . 5 % PFA / 0 . 5 % GA ( III , IV ; Table 1 , Condition # 10 ) . All four images are maximum projections of the z - sections , excluding out - of - focus slices ( i . e . , below and above the cells ) . The box below IV highlights a long nanotube poorly visualized in the general image ( yellow arrowheads ) , since it was detected in the upper three z - sections and passes above the cell marked X Detection of mRNA Transfer Between Mammalian Cells by smFISH 123 2 . To determine if mRNA is in nanotubes , use the “Image” ! “Color” ! “Merge channels” option to merge the FISH and phalloidin - FITC channels . Look for appearance of FISH signal at the x , y , and z of the nanotube . Verify that these spots are also recognized as positive spots by FISH - quant ( Subheading 3 . 5 ) . 3 . To determine if two - color FISH signals colocalize ( e . g . , when trying to assess if the mRNA is full - length , see Subheading 2 . 2 , step 4 ) , use “Merge channels” to merge the two FISH images . Use the “Straight” tool to pass a line through the spot and use “Analyze” ! “Plot proﬁle” to plot the spot intensity along the pixels in both channels . If the spots colocalize , the intensity peaks should colocalize . Verify that these spots are also recog - nized as positive spots by FISH - quant ( Subheading 3 . 5 ) . 4 Notes 1 . Coating with ﬁbronectin increases mRNA transfer , as com - pared to uncoated or polylysine - coated coverslips [ 4 ] . How - ever , some cell types might prefer a different extracellular matrix ( e . g . , collagen ) and the efﬁciency of cell attachment , membrane nanotube production , and mRNA transfer should be determined empirically . 2 . During the preparation of this manuscript , we tested many conditions that were aimed to better preserve membrane nano - tubes ( Table 1 ) . All these conditions included phalloidin - FITC staining , which allowed much better detection of nanotubes and other thin protrusions . Testing a variety of conditions and buffers with 4 % PFA did not improve nanotube preservation ( Table 1 , Conditions # 2 – 9 ) and , in some cases , reduced the SNR of the FISH signal due to increased background staining ( Table 1 , Conditions # 2 , 4 – 6 , 9 ) . Using Subheading 3 . 3 , step 13 slightly improved nanotube preservation and , therefore , we used this step for Conditions # 3 – 29 . A ﬁxative of 3 . 5 % PFA / 0 . 5 % glutaraldehyde ( GA ) has increased the number of preserved nanotubes by three to four fold compared to 4 % PFA ( Table 1 , Condition # 10 ) . GA also improved the preservation of long nanotubes ( > 10 μ m ) compared to 4 % PFA ( Fig . 2 ) . However , GA causes a signiﬁcant increase in autoﬂuorescence at the FITC and Cy3 channels . This can be quenched by 1 mg / mL NaBH 4 ( Table 1 , Condition # 18 ) . Increasing NaBH 4 concentration did not improve the quenching signiﬁcantly , but performing two 10 min incubations , instead of one , did . Note that NaBH 4 should be prepared immediately prior to use . NaBH 4 ﬁzzles like carbonated water , and requires a repeat of Subheading 3 . 3 , step 5 and at least two washes in PBSM 124 Gal Haimovich and Jeffrey E . Gerst ( Subheading 3 . 3 , step 6 ) , which decreases the efﬁciency of nanotube preservation ( from a three to four fold with GA to ~ twofold with NaBH 4 ) . Unexpectedly , transferred mRNA was not detected when cells were ﬁxed with GA , whereas endoge - nous mRNA was readily detected . This was not due to the increased autoﬂuorescence since using a Cy5 - labeled probe or quenching autoﬂuorescence with NaBH 4 did not improve the detection of transferred mRNA ( Table 1 , Conditions # 11 and 18 ; Fig . 3a ) . Reducing GA concentration also had not improved the detection of transferred mRNA ( Fig . 3a ) . We suspected that a distinctive GA cross - linked protein or RNA structure is blocking the hybridization of the probes to the transferred mRNA . We therefore tried multiple treatments Fig . 3 Glutaraldehyde ﬁxation prevents detection of transferred mRNA . ( a ) GA ﬁxation prevents detection of transferred mRNA regardless of GA concentration or quenching with NaBH 4 ( Table 1 , Conditions # 10 , 14 , and 18 ) . The graph shows the number of transferred MBS mRNAs detected in wild - type MEFs cocultured with MBS - MEFs for 3 h . Each dot represents a single cell . ( b ) Treatment with proteinase K ( 1 μ g / mL for 2 or 10 min ) followed by 1 M urea ( Table 1 , Condition # 29 ) allows for the detection of 25 – 30 % of transferred mRNA in GA - ﬁxed acceptor cells in cocultures . The graph shows the number of transferred MBS mRNAs detected in wild - type MEFs cocultured with MBS - MEFs for 3 h . Each dot represents a single cell . ( c ) Average ( + SEM ) number of nanotubes per cell ( as detected by phalloidin - FITC staining ) in the experiment depicted in ( b ) . ( d ) Length of nanotubes from cells measured in panel ( c ) . Each dot represents a single nanotube Detection of mRNA Transfer Between Mammalian Cells by smFISH 125 that were aimed at opening such structures ( Table 1 , Condi - tions # 17 , 19 – 29 ) . Only the treatment with proteinase K ( 1 μ g / mL for 2 – 10 min ) after permeabilization , which was combined with replacement of formamide with urea ( 1 M ) ( Table 1 , Condition # 29 ) allowed for the detection of trans - ferred mRNA , but was three to four fold less than the expected level ( i . e . , when compared to regular FISH ) ( Fig . 3b ) . How - ever , this treatment also reduced the level of nanotube preser - vation ( Fig . 3c , d ) . Our results suggest that a protein “shell” may prevent the FISH probes from accessing the mRNA in samples that are cross - linked with GA . Thus , until the GA cross - linked protein “shell” is identiﬁed , we decided to revert to the regular smFISH protocol as the main approach of this methods paper . However , we made a few minor changes com - pared to the regular smFISH [ 3 ] . In particular , we reduced the number of rinses in Subheadings 3 . 3 , steps 3 and 19 , and added an alternative hybridization step ( Subheading 3 . 3 , step 13 ) . This slightly improves nanotube preservation , but without affecting the smFISH signal . Finally , smFISH with Conditions # 10 or 18 might allow for the investigation of endogenous mRNAs that localize to the base of nanotubes . For more details on the conditions that we tested for nanotube preservation , see Table 1 . 3 . It is recommended to calibrate probe concentration when using probes for the ﬁrst time . The default concentrations are 250 nM or 10 ng / sample . Test a range of concentrations on samples of donor only cells to calibrate probe concentration . Comparing donor alone to acceptor alone can assist in choos - ing the concentration that gives the best spot detection with minimal background in acceptor cells . 4 . There are other commercially available antifade solutions , as well as lab - made antifade solutions that can be used ( e . g . , by using glucose oxidase ) . However , we have no experience with those and these might not be suitable for FISH or for long - term storage . 5 . We typically culture donor and acceptor cells at a 1 : 1 ratio and aim for ~ 90 % conﬂuence at time of ﬁxation . Higher conﬂuence will limit the ability to accurately outline individual cells . In particular , it may result in many occurrences where donor and acceptor cells partially overlap . In such a case , the entire over - lapped region should be excluded from the FISH - quant analy - sis . We were able detect β - actin - MBS mRNA transfer at a lower ratio of donor to acceptor ( even down to 1 : 99 ) or down to 50 % conﬂuence of a 1 : 1 coculture . In these cases , we detected less transferred mRNA . Therefore , the efﬁciency of transfer of the query mRNAs under different ratios / conﬂuence levels should be determined empirically . 126 Gal Haimovich and Jeffrey E . Gerst 6 . We detected β - actin mRNA transfer in MEFs within 30 min after seeding the donor cells on top of pre - seeded acceptor cells , with a peak at ~ 2 . 5 h [ 4 ] . For some mRNAs ( e . g . , β - actin , SV40 large T antigen , HER2 ) coculturing for 3 h or 24 h yielded the same levels of transferred mRNA . 7 . A commercial alternative to the tripods are “Transwell” plates , which allow for a coculture of two cell types separated by a porous membrane . Care should be taken to use “Transwell” plates with pores that will enable ( or exclude ) EVs of particular sizes to pass through . 8 . Do not use a confocal microscope for smFISH imaging . The higher light intensity of the lasers can cause rapid bleaching of the FISH signal . Since the smFISH signal is relatively weak and requires long exposure times to collect enough light , photo - bleaching limits the total amount of light collected . This is particularly problematic when taking multiple z sections in order to create the 3D image stack . 9 . Sometimes false - positive spots in acceptor cells are detected due to inaccurate outlining of the nucleus . In this case , most of the “positive” spots will appear around the nucleus . Nat Methods 14 ( 7 ) : 703 – 706 Detection of mRNA Transfer Between Mammalian Cells by smFISH 129 Chapter 9 Live - Cell Imaging of mRNP – NPC Interactions in Budding Yeast Azra Lari , Farzin Farzam , Pierre Bensidoun , Marlene Oefﬁnger , Daniel Zenklusen , David Grunwald , and Ben Montpetit Abstract Single - molecule resolution imaging has become an important tool in the study of cell biology . Aptamer - based approaches ( e . g . , MS2 and PP7 ) allow for detection of single RNA molecules in living cells and have been used to study various aspects of mRNA metabolism , including mRNP nuclear export . Here we outline an imaging protocol for the study of interactions between mRNPs and nuclear pore complexes ( NPCs ) in the yeast S . cerevisiae , including mRNP export . We describe in detail the steps that allow for high - resolution live - cell mRNP imaging and measurement of mRNP interactions with NPCs using simultaneous two - color imaging . Our protocol discusses yeast strain construction , choice of marker proteins to label the nuclear pore complex , as well as imaging conditions that allow high signal - to - noise data acquisition . Moreover , we describe various aspects of postacquisition image analysis for single molecule tracking and image registra - tion allowing for the characterization of mRNP – NPC interactions . Key words mRNP export , Nuclear pore complex , NPC , Live - cell imaging , Single molecule , Budding yeast , S . cerevisiae , Fluorescent imaging , PP7 , Superregistration 1 Introduction Transport from the nucleus to the cytoplasm is required for mes - senger ribonucleic acids ( mRNAs ) to assemble with ribosomes in the cytoplasm for translation . mRNAs in complex with associated proteins are referred to as messenger ribonucleoprotein particles ( mRNPs ) . Export of mRNPs occurs through nuclear pore com - plexes ( NPCs ) , large protein assemblies imbedded in the double membrane lipid bilayer of the nuclear envelope [ 1 ] . Built from ~ 30 nucleoporin proteins ( Nups ) , NPCs form an eight - fold symmetri - cal assembly that can be divided into three functionally distinct domains . A central scaffold anchors the NPC into the nuclear envelope and forms the central transport channel that allows Yaron Shav - Tal ( ed . ) , Imaging Gene Expression : Methods and Protocols , Methods in Molecular Biology , vol . 2038 , https : / / doi . org / 10 . 1007 / 978 - 1 - 4939 - 9674 - 2 _ 9 , © Springer Science + Business Media , LLC , part of Springer Nature 2019 Azra Lari , Farzin Farzam and Pierre Bensidoun contributed equally to this work . 131 nucleocytoplamic exchange . Attached to the central scaffold are a number of asymmetrically distributed Nups on the nuclear and cytoplasmic side of the NPC . The cytoplasmic asymmetric Nups play a role in mRNP release after translocation through the central channel , a process that requires the dynamic association of the DEAD - box protein Dbp5 ( DDX19b in humans ) with the NPC [ 2 ] . On the nuclear side , long ﬁlamentous proteins , Mlp1 / Mlp2 ( TPR in humans ) , protrude into the nucleoplasm converging in a distal ring structure called the nuclear basket . Mlp1 / 2 , as well as basket associated proteins , interact with mRNPs through associated RNA binding proteins ( RBPs ) to provide a docking site for mRNPs , thereby regulating access to the central transport channel [ 3 , 4 ] . Upon entering the central channel , translocation is not rate limiting , with mRNAs reaching the other side of the nuclear pore within tens of milliseconds [ 5 – 8 ] . The yeast S . cerevisiae has been a valuable model system to study mRNA transport . The combination of proteomic and genetic approaches were instrumental to identify most players in this pro - cess and in the development of current models describing mRNP export [ 9 – 11 ] . However , how this process is coordinated in space and time , or coupled to upstream and downstream events in gene expression , is still largely unexplored . Live cell single molecule microscopy is an important tool to study this process , and when combined with the power of yeast genetics , has the potential to reveal mechanistic details about this process [ 4 , 6 , 12 ] . However , studying mRNP export using high resolution single molecule microscopy is technically challenging . For example , the size of an NPC in relation to diffraction limited imaging makes it difﬁcult to assign the position of a single mRNP to a subregion of the NPC . Gaussian ﬁtting does allow for subdiffraction localization of single mRNPs , but mRNP signals have to be aligned to a reference nuclear pore signal acquired in a different channel . Moreover , a yeast cell possesses a 200 nm thick cell wall that induces signiﬁcant light scattering affecting signal - to - noise ratio and therefore locali - zation precision . Of the various methods allowing for RNA visualization in cells , including molecular beacons and , more recently , variants of Cas9 and Cas13 , not all methods allow for single molecule detection in a living cell under fast imaging regimes [ 13 ] . The PP7 and MS2 RNA labeling approaches are currently the most robust method for single molecule resolution mRNA imaging . These aptamer - based approaches use the high afﬁnity and speciﬁcity of bacteriophage capsid proteins ( CP ) to bind to an RNA stem - loop that can be inserted into mRNAs of interest . Fusion of the CP to a ﬂuorescent protein allows for the tagged RNA to be ﬂuorescently marked and multimerizing CP binding sites increases signal - to - noise ratio , with 12 – 24 repeats being typically used for mRNA labeling and detec - tion in yeast [ 4 , 6 , 14 – 17 ] . 132 Azra Lari et al . To track mRNP – NPC interactions and export events , NPCs are visualized by the labeling of speciﬁc nuclear pore proteins using ﬂuorescent proteins . mRNP export is then measured using a micro - scope setup that allows simultaneous acquisition of both mRNP and NPC signals using laser illumination and sensitive EMCCD cameras for detection . Because of the spectral shift associated with imaging two channels , measuring the interaction of an mRNP with an NPC , and in particular a subregion of the NPC , requires precise registration of the two imaging channels . This is achieved by a combination of mechanical alignment and image processing using a common signal detected on both cameras [ 5 ] . Moreover , mRNP movement must be tracked in each frame using Gaussian ﬁtting with information regarding mRNP movement in prior and subsequent frames used to identify mRNP export events . In this chapter , we describe a detailed protocol to image mRNP transport with high spatial and temporal resolution by combining the PP7 mRNA labeling strategy with ultrafast image acquisition and image registration allowing the study mRNP export in real time . 2 Materials 2 . 1 Yeast Strains and Plasmids 1 .
