Sci Rep 5 : 15915 . https : / / doi . org / 10 . 1038 / srep15915 Visualization of Endogenous Factors Using Antibodies 221 Chapter 15 Monitoring Transcription Factor Oligomerization in Single Living Cells by Number and Brightness Analysis Eugenia Cammarota and Davide Mazza Abstract One key step in the activation of inducible transcription factors is their homooligomerization , which can be measured in individual living cells by a ﬂuorescence microscopy technique called Number and Brightness analysis ( N & B ) . In this chapter we describe how to acquire and analyze confocal microscopy time - series to provide information about transcription factor oligomerization in living cells using this technique . Key words Number and brightness , Oligomerization , Transcription factor , Live cell imaging , Image analysis 1 Introduction In response to speciﬁc external stimuli , inducible transcription factors ( iTFs ) stimulate the transcription of target genes by sequence - speciﬁc binding to responsive elements in their enhan - cers / promoters [ 1 ] . The activation of an iTF is typically a complex process that involves several phenomena that can be potentially regulated independently . First , in response to activating stimuli , the nuclear concentration of eukaryotic iTFs often increases : for example , following induction of hormone receptors , NF - kB trans - locates from the cytoplasm to the nucleus [ 2 ] . For other iTFs , such as the tumor - suppressor p53 , the increase of the nuclear abundancy is achieved by modulating the iTF degradation rate [ 3 ] . In addition to an increase of nuclear abundancy , many iTFs respond to the activating stimulus by homooligomerizing : For example , hormone receptors homodimerize—although higher order oligomers have also been observed [ 4 ] —while p53 tetra - merizes [ 5 , 6 ] . In vitro , the oligomers typically display higher afﬁnity to their speciﬁc binding sites than monomers , potentially providing a functional role to iTF homooligomerization . Further , activated iTFs often accumulate posttranslational modiﬁcations Yaron Shav - Tal ( ed . ) , Imaging Gene Expression : Methods and Protocols , Methods in Molecular Biology , vol . 2038 , https : / / doi . org / 10 . 1007 / 978 - 1 - 4939 - 9674 - 2 _ 15 , © Springer Science + Business Media , LLC , part of Springer Nature 2019 223 that can further modulate their capability to bind responsive ele - ments and to stimulate transcription of their target genes [ 7 , 8 ] . Notably , in the case of p53 we have recently shown that the binding kinetics of p53 to its binding sites are tuned in living cells in response to activating stimuli ( DNA damage ) , possibly as a consequence of a modulation in p53 levels , in p53 oligomerization kinetics and / or in p53 posttranslational modiﬁcations [ 8 ] . Dissecting which and to what extent each of these phenomena contribute to the stimulation of target genes transcription is funda - mental to provide a complete description of the iTF action , and to identify the steps in iTF activation that are compromised in disease . In this context , techniques with single - cell sensitivity are emerging , as they allow capturing the heterogeneity in TF behavior in the cellular population and they provide a means of relating different observables together ( e . g . , TF abundancy , TF oligomerization , and TF binding kinetics ) . Several ﬂuorescence microscopy techniques allow for monitor - ing iTF properties at the single - cell level and have been covered in previous volumes of this book series and elsewhere . Time - lapse imaging of ﬂuorescently tagged TFs can be used to quantify the TF abundancy in living cells [ 9 ] , while techniques such as ﬂuores - cence recovery after photobleaching [ 9 , 10 ] , ﬂuorescence correla - tion spectroscopy [ 11 , 12 ] , and single molecule tracking ( SMT ) [ 8 , 13 ] can provide quantitative estimates of the TF binding rates with single cell sensitivity . Protein oligomerization can be also monitored cell by cell , by a ﬂuorescence ﬂuctuation technique known as number and brightness analysis ( N & B ) . Originally implemented in the 2008 by Digman et al . [ 14 ] , N & B consists in the statistical analysis of intensity ﬂuctuations from time - lapse acquisitions of live cells expressing ﬂuorescently labeled TFs . Such intensity ﬂuctuations contain information about the dynamic of TFs , and their statistical analysis allows to measure the amount of ﬂuorescent light emitted by each complex crossing the observation volume called brightness , ε , as well as average the number of complexes n in the observation volume ( see Note 1 ) . By comparing the brightness of our complex of interest with the brightness measured for a control protein with known oligomeri - zation state ( e . g . , monomeric ﬂuorescent tag ) , it is possible to estimate the oligomerization state of our protein of interest [ 6 , 15 ] . For example , we can consider having two samples with the same concentration of ﬂuorescent molecules : in the ﬁrst sample the molecules are in monomeric form , while in the second sample the molecules are bound as dimers . Under these conditions , the two samples will generate images with the same average ﬂuores - cence intensity . If the molecules can move in and out of the obser - vation volume , the ﬂuctuations in the ﬂuorescence intensity will be higher for the dimeric sample than for the monomeric one , as each dimer moving in and out of the observation volume brings a 224 Eugenia Cammarota and Davide Mazza ﬂuctuation in the ﬂuorescent signal that is twice the ﬂuctuation brought by a monomer ( Fig . 1a ) . Information about the brightness ε , of the “diffusing unit” in the sample can be therefore calculated by measuring the average and the variance of the ﬂuorescence signal over time ( pixelwise , Fig . 1b ) , and a dimer with both units ﬂuores - cently labelled will provide a brightness that is twice the brightness of a monomer ( see Note 2 , Fig . 1c , d ) . Other than N & B , protein oligomerization can be measured with different techniques . coimmunoprecipitation ( Co - IP ) , for instance , is a biochemical mean that uses protein speciﬁc antibodies to identify particular targets , and the complexes isolated in this way can then be analyzed to identify the binding partners . Co - IP is a population assay and as such does not give access to the full distri - bution of single cell behaviors . N & B on the other side , not only has Fig . 1 ( a ) Representation of the time trace of intensity ﬂuctuations arising from the diffusion of labeled proteins across the observation volume . The average of the signal is directly linked to the protein concentration while a higher oligomerization state produces a higher variance of the signal . ( b ) The data are treated as a time stack of images . Brightness ( ε ) and number ( n ) are calculated for each pixel ( ij ) from the time average ( < k > ) and variance ( σ 2 ) of the intensity . ( c ) Fluorescence image , numbers and brightness map of monomeric and dimeric mutants of the transcription factor p53 . The two samples have similar intensity levels , but in the monomeric case there are more particles with lower brightness compared to the dimeric sample , where there are less oligomers but with higher brightness . ( d ) Brightness distribution of a monomeric and dimeric sample . Each dot represents the brightness of a different cell calculated by averaging the values of the brightness map over ﬁve different rectangles belonging to the region of interest ( the nucleus of the cell in this example ) TF Oligomerization in Live Cells by N & B Analysis 225 the advantage of being a single cell method but it actually reaches a resolution equal to the pixel dimension in the image , meaning that it is possible to build a brightness map and monitor the aggregation level in the different compartments of the cell ( Fig . 1c ) . Among the microscopy methods used to test proteins interac - tion , Fo¨rster resonance energy transfer ( FRET ) and especially its variant homo - FRET can also be used to provide homooligomeriza - tion maps within the cell [ 16 , 17 ] . Homo - FRET detection however requires the use of ad hoc microscopy instrumentation capable of measuring ﬂuorescence anisotropy pixelwise , while N & B data can , in principle , be acquired with any ﬂuorescence microscope . Super - resolution localization microscopy , and ﬁxed - cell step photobleaching analysis can also be used to quantify oligomeriza - tion / clustering of proteins [ 18 , 19 ] , but differently from N & B , these approaches are typically limited to in vitro analyses or ﬁxed samples . In this protocol we will describe how to quantify TF oligomeri - zation using N & B . The workﬂow requires the transfection / infec - tion of the cellular model with a ﬂuorescently tagged version of the TF of interest [ Subheading 3 . 1 ] . Next , we describe the collection of N & B data , using a confocal microscope with suitable detectors and light sources [ Subheading 3 . 2 ] . The N & B data is then corrected for photobleaching and cellular motion and analyzed , to provide quan - titative information about the abundancy and the apparent bright - ness state of the oligomers diffusing in the nuclear environment [ Subheading 3 . 3 ] . Comparison of these brightness values with standards in a known oligomerization state , allows monitoring TF oligomerization and TF nuclear concentration cell by cell . 2 Materials 2 . 1 Sample Preparation N & B experiments can be used to monitor the relative brightness of any ﬂuorescently labelled macromolecule , provided that the mole - cule of interest is free to diffuse in and out of the observation volume ( see Note 1 ) during the time of the whole acquisition [ 14 ] . In biological samples , N & B is typically used to quantify the oligomerization state of proteins . In this case , it is important to have the vast majority of the copies of the protein of interest ﬂuores - cently tagged , in order to avoid having oligomers in which only a fraction of the subunits is labeled . This can be achieved by transfect - ing the labelled protein of interest in cell lines where the endoge - nous protein is knocked out / silenced . An emerging alternative is the application of CRISPR - Cas9 technology [ 20 ] to generate knockin cell lines where the sequence encoding for the ﬂuorescent tag is appended at the C - terminus of the protein of interest . This last method has also the advantage of having the tagged protein of interest expressed at physiological levels ( see Note 3 ) . 226 Eugenia Cammarota and Davide Mazza Regarding the choice of the ﬂuorescent tag , N & B has been typically performed on eGFP ( or other ﬂuorescent proteins , FPs ) - labeled macromolecules . While being the most common choice , FPs have some caveats : GFPs can form weak dimers : therefore , it is advisable to use mutated versions of GFP that show reduced homo - oligomerization , such as the mGFP mutant [ 21 ] . Also , chromo - phore maturation for FPs can be slow ( in the range of hours ) . If the turnover of the protein of interest is expected to be rapid , it is advisable to use FPs with rapid maturation times ( e . g . , mVenus ) . Finally , the photostability of the FPs chromophore is limited , and acquisition settings need to be carefully optimized in order to minimize photobleaching during the N & B acquisition . An alternative to FPs , is represented by posttranslational label - ing systems such as the HaloTag [ 22 ] , where the protein of interest is fused to a nonﬂuorescent receptor , and the labeling is performed by the addition of a cell - permeable ﬂuorescent ligand . As ﬂuores - cent ligands for HaloTag are essentially functionalized organic ﬂuorophores , their brightness and photostability can be much higher than what observed for FPs [ 22 , 23 ] . Further , the HaloTag receptor does not show any detectable homodimerization and folds rapidly following synthesis , overcoming the main problems asso - ciated to FPs . Finally , posttranslational systems are the labeling method of choice for intranuclear SMT , which might be advanta - geous for multiplexed experiments combining this approach with N & B . In the following we will describe the transfection of HaloTag - fused proteins into cell lines that are KO for the endogenous protein . The tagged protein has to be labeled with saturating con - centrations of the ﬂuorescent ligand ( see Note 4 ) in order to ensure that all the fusion proteins will be ﬂuorescently labeled . The mate - rials needed for HaloTag transfection and following labeling are ( for a 500 μ L chamber ) : 1 . Plasmid of the protein of interest expressing protein fused with HaloTag ( 500 ng ) . 2 . HaloTag - JF549 ﬂuorescent ligand ( JFS 549 1 μ M ) . 3 . Lipofectamine 2000 . 4 . Opti - MEM medium . 5 . PBS . 6 . DMEM cell culture medium . 7 . Lab - Tek glass bottom chambered cover glass . 2 . 2 Imaging Instrumentation N & B data acquisition can be performed , in principle , on any ﬂuo - rescence microscope , laser scanning or camera based , provided that some requirement on the light sources and on the detector are satisﬁed . First , the intensity of the light source used for stimulating TF Oligomerization in Live Cells by N & B Analysis 227 ﬂuorescence needs to be stable over time . Second , the detector needs to behave as a photon - counting detector , otherwise the data collected needs to be corrected for the ampliﬁcation noise and offset signal of the detector . Also , nonlinear effects in non - photon - counting detectors can arise [ 24 – 26 ] . Often , modern con - focal microscopes often use photon - counting detectors or hybrid detectors that can be operated in photon counting mode . We describe in Note 5 the controls allowing to verify that the intensity light source is constant , and that the detector responds as a photon - counter . Also having a microscope with optical sectioning capabilities ( e . g . , a confocal , a two - photon excitation , or a total internal reﬂec - tion ﬂuorescence microscope ) is beneﬁcial , since a high background intensity would introduce a bias in the measurement , decreasing the measured brightness . The key microscope components used for our NB data acquisition are : 1 . A white light laser with tunable excitation wavelength . 2 . A commercial confocal microscope . 3 . Software for automatic hardware control and data acquisition . 4 . A hybrid photon counting detector . 5 . A second detector for bright ﬁeld reference image acquisition . 6 . A high numerical aperture objective ( e . g . , (cid:1) 63 oil immersion NA 1 . 4 ) . 7 . An environmental chamber for live sample imaging with tem - perature and CO 2 concentration control ( 37 (cid:3) C and 5 % CO 2 ) . 2 . 3 Software for the Analysis of N & B Data The necessary software to analyze N & B data calculates pixel - wise the variance and the mean of the acquired time - lapse movies . It also includes detrending algorithms to correct for cell movement and ﬂuorescence bleaching . It is quite common for research groups to use their own home built N & B analysis routine . Some of these groups have shared their software online and they can be down - loaded free of charge . Some of these are : ImFCS ( http : / / www . dbs . nus . edu . sg / lab / BFL / imfcs _ image _ j _ plugin . html ) , SimFCS ( https : / / www . lfd . uci . edu / globals / ) , and Nandb ( https : / / rorynolan . github . io / nandb / ) . We wrote a Matlab routine to correct for rigid cell movement , photobleaching , and to perform the N & B analysis . The hardware necessary to run a N & B data analysis with our software consists of : ( a ) A computer with more than 2 GB of RAM . ( b ) Licensed Matlab software ( Requires Image Processing Toolbox ) . ( c ) Our NB analysis routine that can be found here : https : / / github . com / Eugenia00 / NB - analyzer . 228 Eugenia Cammarota and Davide Mazza 3 Methods 3 . 1 Sample Preparation 3 . 1 . 1 Cell Transfection 1 . Plate cells in a Lab - Tek chamber and let them adhere to the glass surface . Cells should be at 80 % conﬂuency at the moment of transfection . 2 . For a 1 . 8 cm 2 well dilute 500 ng of plasmid in Opti - MEM to have a ﬁnal volume of 25 μ L . Mix with the pipette and incubate for 5 min at room temperature . 3 . In the meantime , mix 1 μ L of Lipofectamine in 24 μ L Opti - MEM . Mix with the pipette and incubate for 5 min at room temperature . 4 . Add the solution with the plasmid to the Lipofectamine solu - tion , mix with the pipette and wait 20 min at room temperature . 5 . Change the cell medium with 450 μ L Opti - MEM and add the solution of plasmid and Lipofectamine . Mix and incubate the cells for 8 – 15 h at 37 (cid:3) C , 5 % CO 2 . Change the cell medium with phenol red - free DMEM . 3 . 1 . 2 Labeling of the HaloTag Construct 1 . Mix the HaloTag ligand in phenol red - free cell medium to a ﬁnal concentration of 1 μ M . 2 . Change the medium of the cell - containing Lab - Tek with ligand solution and incubate for 30 min 37 (cid:3) C , 5 % CO 2 . During this time the ligand permeates through the cell membrane and conjugates to the fusion protein . 3 . Wash ﬁve times with PBS and add phenol red free cell medium . Incubate for 15 min at 37 (cid:3) C , 5 % CO 2 . 4 . Again , wash ﬁve times with PBS and add cell medium . Incubate for 15 min at 37 (cid:3) C , 5 % CO 2 . 5 . Change the cell medium one last time and bring the cells to the microscope . 3 . 2 Data Acquisition 3 . 2 . 1 Testing Before starting the data acquisition from the sample it is advisable to run some tests . The ﬁrst test to run is on the detector to check the real response to a set of different light intensities as described in Note 5 . The outcome of this check will have to be taken in consideration during the data analysis . Once the samples are ready , we proceed with the acquisition of the time - lapse movies . 1 . Mount the environmental chamber for live cell imaging on the microscope stage . 2 . Turn on the microscope , the laser and open the software for the microscope and data control . 3 . Put a drop of oil on the (cid:1) 60 oil immersion objective . 4 . Place the sample chamber on the microscope stage . TF Oligomerization in Live Cells by N & B Analysis 229 3 . 2 . 2 Acquiring the Data To have enough statistics , the time series of images need to contain at least 250 – 300 frames . To avoid extra variance terms caused by the cell movement the acquisition has to be as fast as possible , but it also has to satisfy other time constraints : the pixel dwell time has to be short enough so that the intensity ﬂuctuations are not averaged out , in other words it has to be shorter than the time particles take to diffuse over the length scale of a pixel . Another constraint is that the time between consecutive frames has to be long enough , so the intensity is not correlated over time . The diffusion coefﬁcients of transcription factors in the cytosol and in the nucleus have been reported to be on the order of 1 – 10 μ m 2 / s . The following settings on a Leica SP8 confocal scanning microscope are compatible with both cytoplasmic and nuclear proteins . 1 . Image size 512 (cid:1) 512 pixels . 2 . Scanning speed 200 Hz ( 200 lines / s ) in bidirectional mode . 3 . On our microscope these settings provide a pixel size equal to 180 μ m , and a pixel dwell time equal to 2 . 43 μ s . 4 . Pinhole aperture 1 AU . 5 . Number of frames 300 minimizing frame interval for a total of 3 min 15 s . 6 . The white light laser is used with current set at 70 % and intensity set at 5 % ( corresponding to a power of 16 μ W , measured at the back aperture of the objective ) . The wave - length of the laser is set to 561 nm . 7 . The ﬂuorescent channel is acquired with hybrid photon count - ing detector . 8 . Turning on the gating ( 3 . 5 ns ) can be useful to cut out the laser reﬂection on the coverslip . 9 . The bright ﬁeld reference channel is acquired with a photomultiplier . 10 . The window of wavelength acquisition needs to be adjusted depending on the dye . After the ﬁrst two or three acquisitions always check that the bleaching does not exceed 20 % over the time course of the experi - ment . Higher bleaching rates are difﬁcult to correct and introduce an extra variance term . This settings with the HaloTag ligand JF549 typically results in less than 10 % bleaching over the whole time - course . 3 . 3 Data Analysis Once the data acquisition is complete we move the acquired ﬁles to the workstation for the analysis . Create a new folder which will contain all the Matlab scripts . Copy into it the functions down - loaded from here ( https : / / github . com / Eugenia00 / NB - analyzer ) and add the function to upload the . lif ﬁles directly to Matlab 230 Eugenia Cammarota and Davide Mazza ( https : / / www . openmicroscopy . org / bio - formats / downloads / ) . Add the newly created folder to the Matlab path with the “addpath” function or make it your Matlab current folder with the navigation tools . Open the routine Matlab NB _ analyzer . m in the Matlab editor . Our Matlab routine is divided into sections . To run each sec - tion , click on it and use the “Run Section” tool , doing so all the commands within that section will be executed . After loading the images and selecting the region of interest it is possible to correct for cell movement , and to use the boxcar method [ 27 ] to correct the data for bleaching . 1 . Load . lif ﬁle : Change the ﬁrst line with the full path of the . lif ﬁle to be opened and click the “Run Section” button . The name of the series will be displayed . 2 . Select series : change the value of the series number and run the section . The ﬂuorescent and bright ﬁeld channels are loaded and shown to the user . 3 . Region of interest : with the mouse make a squared selection around the region of interest ( e . g . , the nucleus of a cell ) . 4 . Correct for cell movement ( Fig . 2 ) . Running this section , it is possible to correct for cell movement . The movement correc - tion routine runs with different starting parameters . Depend - ing on how much the cell moves , a set of parameters may be more appropriate than others ( see Note 6 ) . At the end of the execution , a new window opens with a set of brightness maps , each one corresponding to the correction performed with a different set of parameters . The user can then select the better performing correction , which can be identiﬁed as the one with less border effects ( see Note 7 ) . If the movement correction fails ( such as in the case of nonrigid movements of the nucleus ) the user can decide to skip the movement correction step or to discard that particular dataset . 5 . Correct for photobleaching : change the size of the boxcar ﬁlter [ 27 ] ( see Note 8 ) to correct for photobleaching . If you do not want to use this tool , specify the boxcar dimension as the total number of frames in the movie . 6 . Calculate N & B parameters in sub regions of the nucleus : when running this section , a window with the bright ﬁeld image of the region of interest appears and the user has to select with the mouse 5 rectangular regions within the region of interest pos - sibly far apart between them and away from evident inhomo - geneities and from the nuclear periphery . The brightness and number values are calculated averaging the relative values rela - tive to the pixels within the ﬁve selections . Other than giving brightness and number estimates , more details of the analysis are saved in the output variable “ res ” This variable contains the TF Oligomerization in Live Cells by N & B Analysis 231 values of number and brightness with and without the boxcar ﬁlter correction , their errors , the boxcar size , the fraction of ﬂuorescence intensity lost due to photobleaching , the position of the region of interest and of the ﬁve selections , the para - meters used for the movement correction and the average bright ﬁeld and ﬂuorescent intensity over time . Every time this section runs it adds a line containing all these outputs to the res variable . To run a different series from the same ﬁle , repeat steps 2 – 5 . To analyze a different region of the same series , repeat steps 3 – 5 . If the cell or the ﬁeld of view does not move at all , step 4 can be skipped . After the analysis of a statistical sample of cells you can use Matlab to plot the results or double click on the variable in the workspace section and copy and paste the content in Excel . It is the case to mention here that brightness values are not usually used as absolute measures , but they need to be compared with other measures on a sample where the protein of interest is in monomeric or in another known oligomeric form . Fig . 2 ( a ) To avoid extra - variance contributions to the intensity ﬂuctuation caused by the cell movement , the cell movement is corrected readjusting , through rigid transformation ( rotations and translations ) , the position of the cell at t > 0 to ﬁt the position of the ﬁrst frame ( t ¼ 0 ) . The best ﬁt is found maximizing the correlation over a full set of transformations . ( b ) Nonrigid movements and bleaching can be further corrected with the boxcar method where number and brightness are calculated within a moving window and then averaged altogether . The variance of signals with an overall modulation of intensity ( such as those caused by cell movement or photobleaching , red curve ) is much higher compared to the same signal without modulation ( black curve ) . The variance can be reduced ( σ 2window ) looking at the data within smaller windows . ( c ) Effect of cell movement on the brightness map on the original data . The extra brightness at the borders ( caused by the nucleus moving during the time - lapse is canceled thanks to the movement correction and the boxcar method 232 Eugenia Cammarota and Davide Mazza 4 Notes 1 . In a laser scanning microscope , the observation volume corre - sponds to the volume within the sample which is illuminated step by step during the scan . Its exact shape is given by the optics of the setup and by the aperture of the confocal pinhole and it is called the point spread function ( PSF ) . For a wideﬁeld microscope , the ﬂuorescence emitted from the sample is pro - jected by the microscope lenses onto the sensor . Each pixel on the ﬁnal image will correspond to different positions . In this case , the observation volume is deﬁned in x - y by the convolu - tion of the microscope PSF for the physical size of the pixel in the sample plane , while the dimension of the observation vol - ume in z are given by the PSF . The intensity emitted by all the particles in the observation volume is recorded on the corresponding pixel of the image . For N & B data acquisition , we require that the particles move enough to diffuse out of the observation volume between consecutive frames in order to generate ﬂuctuations in the recorded intensity . 2 . Numbers and brightness of the tagged oligomers can be measured by estimating the average and the variance of the ﬂuorescence signal over time . The variance of the signal σ 2 is expressed by the sum of two terms . The ﬁrst is related to the movement of the particles σ 2n ( occupation noise ) while the other takes in account the detector noise σ 2d : σ 2 ¼ σ 2n þ σ 2d . In case the detector used is a photon counting one , both terms follow the statistics of Poisson and can be written as function of just the number and brightness of the ﬂuorescent motile units : σ 2n ¼ ε 2 n and σ 2n ¼ ε n . Rearranging these expressions , number ( n ) and brightness ( ε ) can be expressed as [ 14 ] : n ¼ k h i 2 σ 2 (cid:4) k h i , ε ¼ σ 2 (cid:4) k h i k h i : 3 . CRISPR - Cas9 based strategies allow labeled proteins to be expressed at endogenous levels . Although the generation of knock - in cell lines could be challenging , it brings several advan - tages : a complete substitution of the endogenous protein with its tagged version , a homogeneous expression among the cell population , and expression levels close to the endogenous ones , all desirable characteristics for performing NB analysis . It is the case to highlight here the importance of having endogenous expression levels , when measuring TF Oligomerization in Live Cells by N & B Analysis 233 homooligomerization . For example , it is easy to show that the increase in expression levels of a TF shifts the equilibrium of the oligomerization process : given the reversible processes M ½ (cid:5) þ M ½ (cid:5) ! k þ D ½ (cid:5) , M ½ (cid:5) þ M ½ (cid:5) k (cid:4) D ½ (cid:5) ( with [ M ] and [ D ] concentration of monomers and dimers , and k + and k (cid:4) rates of association and dissociation ) the monomer concentration is described by the equation d M ½ (cid:5) dt ¼ (cid:4) k þ M ½ (cid:5) 2 þ 2 k (cid:4) D ½ (cid:5) . At the equilibrium we get D ½ (cid:5) ¼ k þ 2 k (cid:4) M ½ (cid:5) 2 , indicating that the number of dimers increases as the square of the concentration of monomers : therefore , at higher expression levels , the equilibrium will be shifted toward dimers ( Fig . 3a ) . 4 . Halo - Tag technology allows the control of protein labeling fraction enabling different kinds of experiments on the same cellular system . N & B experiments require a fully labeled pro - tein population to avoid an apparent mixed brightness of monomers and oligomers . To decide on the right ligand con - centration , we prepared different samples at different concen - trations and then measured the average ﬂuorescent intensity over about 30 cells per sample , and then plotted the average intensity vs . ligand concentration . The concentration for which the intensity has reached a plateau corresponds to the satura - tion of the tagged proteins ( Fig . 3b ) . 5 . Depending on the kind of detector used , one may need to correct the signal for offset and gain and therefore use different equations to calculate the numbers and brightness estimates . If the detector can operate in photon - counting mode , the signal Fig . 3 ( a ) The fraction of the different monomer vs . dimeric species changes as function of the total protein concentration , calculated for a reversible dimerization reaction . High expression levels are enough to change the balance in favor of oligomers . ( b ) Amount of labeled protein as a function of the ligand concentration . The optimal ligand concentration for N & B experiments corresponds to the saturation of the available sites . ( c ) The characteristic Poisson distribution of a signal acquired with a photon counting detector can be veriﬁed plotting time variance vs . average . The data plotted are acquired from a ﬁxed sample and averaged over pixels with similar intensity . Photon - counting detectors should provide a linear relationship with slope equal to one and intercept equal to zero 234 Eugenia Cammarota and Davide Mazza recorded does not need additional corrections and N & B esti - mates can be calculated as described in Note 2 . A photon counting detector is a high sensitive device able to detect and count signals as low as a single photon , Thanks to this “count - ing” property , such detectors preserve the Poisson nature of the process of counting the number of photons hitting the sensor in a given time interval ( pixel dwell time ) . To characterize the response of the detector we acquire movies of an immobile sample at different intensities ( also a scratch on the bottom of a Petri dish or a reﬂection can serve for the purpose ) . For each pixel we then calculate the mean and variance of the signal over time and build a plot of the mean vs . the variance of the signal . A photon - counting detec - tor should provide a linear relationship between the two vari - ables , with zero offset and slope equal to 1 ( Fig . 3c ) . A second test that has to be run is about the stability of the illumination power . It is critical that the laser source has con - stant emission over time . Indeed , additional intensity ﬂuctua - tions as well as a steady increase or decrease would result in an overestimation of the brightness . An overall drop or increase of power over much longer times would also compromise the reproducibility of the experiment . To check the stability of the laser source we acquired a time series , about 100 images over 5 min , of a ﬁxed sample . To not have bleaching issues we used a scratch on a plastic dish , we illuminated it with the same source used for the N & B experiments and acquired a very large wavelength spectrum . We then calculate for every image the average overall intensity . The illumination can be considered sufﬁciently stable if the ﬂuctuations are smaller than 5 % . The test can also be repeated on different days or at different times after the illumination source was turned on to compare the overall light source stability over longer times . 6 . The movement correction is achieved by correlating the image at time zero with the images at later times , upon a full set of rigid transformations combining rotations and translations ( Fig . 2a ) . The transformation chosen is the one providing the highest correlation value . As individual images in the time series might have low signal - to - background ratio , a more reli - able result is obtained if we divide the sequence in sections composed by a number of frames n frames , average the images in each section and then correlate pairs of images that are a number of sections apart ( Δ t section ) : The software will perform the transformation for a number of different values for the parameters ( n frames , Δ t section ) and the user is given the possibil - ity to choose the set of parameters that provide the better correction for cell movement , as described in Note 7 . TF Oligomerization in Live Cells by N & B Analysis 235 7 . Neighboring pixels at the cell edge and at the nucleus – cyto - plasm boundary can display high difference in intensity ( e . g . , the pixels mapping the cell nucleus can be much brighter than those mapping the cytoplasm ) . Therefore , if the cell and / or the nucleus move , the pixels relative to these edge regions will record large intensity ﬂuctuations over time ( Fig . 2c ) . Conse - quently , the extra variance term due to the movement will increase the local brightness and will result in artiﬁcially high values around these edge regions , along the direction of the movement . The right parameters for the correction of the cell movement can therefore be chosen as the ones that minimize these edge effects . 8 . The boxcar [ 27 ] is a method to correct the measured bright - ness for the photobleaching effect . It consists of calculating the moving average and moving variance , calculating the bright - ness in every window and then averaging them together . The boxcar dimension corresponds to the size of the moving win - dow . Compared to other detrending algorithms it has the advantage to be model free , but on the other hand the result depends on the boxcar dimension . A too small window may cut out good intensity oscillations and on the other hand a too big one may preserve spurious ones . Nolan et al . [ 28 ] have recently published a method to correctly decide the boxcar size . Acknowledgments We are grateful to Dr . Valeria Caiolfa and Dr . Moreno Zamai for their support on N & B analysis . Bioin - formatics 33 : 3508 – 3510 . https : / / doi . org / 10 . 1093 / bioinformatics / btx434 TF Oligomerization in Live Cells by N & B Analysis 237 Chapter 16 Visualizing Transcription Factor Binding on Mitotic Chromosomes Using Single - Molecule Live - Cell Imaging James Z . J . Kwan , Thomas F . Nguyen , and Sheila S . Teves Abstract For over two decades , scientists have observed that most transcription factors ( TFs ) become excluded from mitotic chromosomes of mammalian cells undergoing cell division . The few TFs that were observed to remain bound to chromosomes have been termed mitotic bookmarkers and were predicted to play important roles in reestablishing transcription after mitosis . Using live - cell imaging of endogenous TFs in mouse embryonic stem cells , we discovered that the observed exclusion from mitotic chromosomes is largely a result of formaldehyde cross - linking and that in fact , most TFs bind to mitotic chromosomes throughout mitosis . Here , we describe the single - molecule live - cell imaging and analytical tools we used to characterize and quantify TF diffusion and binding as mouse embryonic stem cells proceed through mitosis . Key words Transcription factors , Mouse embryonic stem cells , Mitotic chromosomes , Single - mole - cule live - cell imaging , Dynamics , Residence time , Diffusion 1 Introduction Maintenance of transcription programs is important for cell iden - tity , but this process is challenged during mitosis . As cells divide , the transcriptional machinery is largely inactivated [ 1 – 3 ] and chro - matin becomes highly condensed [ 4 ] . Mammalian cells also disas - semble the nuclear membrane during mitosis , leading to the dispersal of nuclear factors [ 5 – 8 ] . After mitosis , how do daughter cells restart their transcriptional program faithfully ? A proposed mechanism has been termed mitotic bookmarking [ 9 ] , stemming from the observation that transcription factor ( TF ) binding sites in promoters and enhancers remain accessible during mitosis [ 7 ] . However , decades of research have shown that most TFs become evicted from chromosomes during mitosis , leading to the mystery of how these “bookmarked” sites are maintained . Using single - molecule live - cell imaging , we and others recently showed that , contrary to previous literature , many TFs in mouse embryonic Yaron Shav - Tal ( ed . ) , Imaging Gene Expression : Methods and Protocols , Methods in Molecular Biology , vol . 2038 , https : / / doi . org / 10 . 1007 / 978 - 1 - 4939 - 9674 - 2 _ 16 , © Springer Science + Business Media , LLC , part of Springer Nature 2019 239 stem cells ( mESCs ) remain associated with mitotic chromosomes in live conditions and that the exclusion of these TFs was largely due to formaldehyde cross - linking ( Fig . 1 ) [ 10 , 11 , 12 ] . Here , we describe the single - molecule imaging tools and analyses that were crucial in making this discovery and in further understanding TF behavior in mESCs at different stages of the cell cycle . A key requirement for single - molecule imaging of TFs at dif - ferent cell cycle stages is generating mESCs containing two tagged proteins . First , we stably integrated an H2B - GFP construct into mouse embryonic stem cells ( mESCs ) , which allowed for efﬁcient identiﬁcation of mitotic cells ( Fig . 1 ) . Second , in the H2B - GFP expressing mESCs , we generated an endogenous knockin of the HaloTag [ 13 ] into our TF of interest using established CRISPR / Cas9 technology [ 14 ] . HaloTag is a modiﬁed bacterial protein capable of covalently binding to a synthetic ﬂuorophore for better and longer single - molecule live - cell imaging [ 13 , 15 ] . Endogenous tagging also ensures that the tagged protein is both functional and present in physiological levels to avoid overexpression biases [ 10 , 16 ] . However , if endogenous - tagging is not feasible , low overexpression of the HaloTag - TF fusion is also possible , providing that control experiments are performed to validate that the over - expressed factor functions similarly as the endogenous protein . Fig . 1 Cellular ﬁxation leads to exclusion of TFs from mitotic chromosomes . Top panel shows cells with the overexpressed Halo - Sox2 transcription factor and the bottom panel shows endogenously tagged Halo - Sox2 both labeled with JF549 dye . Cells were ﬁxed using 4 % paraformaldehyde followed by standard immunoﬂuorescence using α - Sox2 . Halo - Sox2 signal was visualized using both the JF - 549 dye ( middle panel ) and the anti - Sox2 signal ( right panel ) , with mitotic chromosomes marked with H2B - GFP shown on the left . Image recreated from [ 10 ] 240 James Z . J . Kwan et al . Once the mESCs of interest are generated , cells can be prepared for different single - molecule imaging modalities . For controls , a cell line stably expressing the HaloTag is needed to approximate full diffusion , and a cell line stably expressing a HaloTag fused to the histone H2B ( H2B - Halo ) is needed to approximate long - lived chromatin binding . The second requirement for single - molecule imaging of TFs is a specialized total internal reﬂection ﬂuorescence ( TIRF ) micro - scope , with capabilities for live - cell imaging . For our purposes of visualizing TF – chromatin interactions , we used this TIRF micro - scope in two ways , fast - and slow - tracking modes . The fast - tracking mode allows us to answer questions such as : ( 1 ) what percent of TF molecules are bound on mitotic chromosomes versus freely diffus - ing ? And ( 2 ) what are the diffusion coefﬁcients of the bound and diffusing populations ? To perform fast - tracking experiments , we labeled our mESCs with saturating concentrations of a photoacti - vatable Halo - ligand ( PA - JF646 ) so that the vast majority of Halo - TF is conjugated with the ﬂuorescent dye . We then used the Acousto - Optic Tunable Filters ( AOTF ) to deliver short ( 1 ms ) stroboscopic pulses of the 633 nm laser at maximal laser intensity to minimize motion blur , and acquired images every 7 ms for a total of 20 , 000 – 30 , 000 frames ( Fig . 2 ) . In between acquisition stages , a short burst of 405 nm laser ( 0 . 45 ms ) at low laser intensity was used to photoactivate molecules . The fast acquisition rates combined with low photoactivation means that , on average , less than 1 mole - cule is visible per frame , allowing for unambiguous tracking of the same molecule as it moves and binds DNA over time . After acqui - sition , we used SLIMfast [ 10 , 17 – 19 ] , an established computa - tional tool for localization and tracking of individual particles , to Fig . 2 Schematic of stroboscopic illumination and photoactivation during fast - tracking mode used to localize and identify molecules via SLIMfast and to determine their jump lengths via Spot - On . Using AOTF , short stroboscopic pulses of the 633 nm laser were delivered and images were acquired every 4 – 7 ms for 20 , 000 – 30 , 000 frames . In between the burst of 633 nm lasers , 405 nm laser was used at low intensity to photoactivate individual molecules . The molecules are then localized and tracked using SLIMfast and jump lengths were determined using Spot - On . Image recreated from [ 20 ] Imaging Transcription Factor Binding on Mitotic Chromosomes 241 output trajectories of each particle over time . We then used a newly developed computational tool called Spot - on [ 20 ] to calculate the distance between locations across different frames , plot the distri - bution of measured distances after a speciﬁed number of frames , and ﬁt a dynamic displacement model that would provide estimates on the fraction of bound verses freely diffusing molecules and the associated diffusion coefﬁcients . For example , in our analysis of TF binding during mitosis , we quantiﬁed that about 30 % of the TF TBP binds chromatin during interphase , and that this bound pop - ulation decreases to about 15 % during mitosis ( Fig . 3 ) . We also performed single - molecule imaging in the slow - tracking mode , which allows us to focus on the bound population and answer questions such as : ( 1 ) how many TFs are binding nonspeciﬁcally ( transient events that are largely sequence - independent ) versus in a speciﬁc manner ( longer events that are mainly sequence - speciﬁc interactions ) ? And ( 2 ) for how long do TFs bind on mitotic chromosomes ? To perform slow - tracking experiments , we labeled our mESCs with low concentrations of standard ﬂuorescent Halo - ligand ( JF - 549 ) and acquired images every 500 ms for a total of 2400 – 3000 frames ( ~ 20 min ) . During this much slower acquisition rate , we maintain constant but low laser excitation power . The result of this imaging technique is that bound molecules remain as diffraction - limited spots whereas Fig . 3 Scatter plot representing fraction of bound TBP molecules in interphase compared to mitosis . A three state kinetic model was ﬁtted to the data which represents three distinct population : a fast - diffusing TBP population ( “fast” ) , a slower - diffusing population where TBP may be in a complex with TFIID ( “slow” ) , and a stably bound population where TBP is bound on to DNA . mESC cells expressing just the HaloTag ( Halo ) was used as a control . We estimate that 27 . 1 % of TBP molecules are bound in interphase and this decreases to 13 . 3 % in mitosis . Each point represents a single cell ( n ¼ 32 ) over four biological replicates and ∗∗∗∗ p - value < 0 . 0001 . Image recreated from [ 17 ] 242 James Z . J . Kwan et al . diffusing molecules blur out . As with the fast - tracking mode , we localized and tracked each diffraction - limited “bound” TF using SLIMfast . We then used custom MATLAB codes to perform resi - dence time analysis as previously described [ 10 , 17 , 21 ] . In this analysis , we calculate the number of frames , and hence the length of time , we can detect a “bound” TF in the same location . We then plot a survival curve of the percent of molecules that are detectable as a function of time ( Fig . 4 ) and ﬁt a two - component exponential decay model to extract the empirical off - rates of speciﬁc and non - speciﬁc binding . The empirical off rate is the sum of the photo - bleaching rate and the actual off - rate . To estimate the photobleaching rate , and therefore calculate the actual off - rate , we used the empirical off - rate of H2B - Halo because , as a histone protein , H2B displays very little unbinding [ 10 ] . We can then calculate the average residence time of the bound population , which is the inverse of the photobleaching - corrected off - rate . For theoretical details on residence time analysis of tracked particles , see [ 10 , 21 ] . For Matlab codes to implement SLIMfast and residence time analysis , see [ 10 ] . Below , we provide a detailed procedure for performing both fast - and slow - tracking modes of single - molecule live - cell imaging , and the corresponding analyses . These experi - mental tools provide an unprecedented view of the diffusion and DNA - binding dynamics of transcription factors in live cells . Fig . 4 Survival curve for endogenously tagged Halo - Sox2 in interphase ( gray ) versus mitosis ( red ) with representative images shown . A two - component exponential decay model representing speciﬁc vs . nonspeciﬁc binding was used to ﬁt the survival curve to obtain the relative residence time on the DNA ( inset ) . Quantiﬁcation of the residence time in mitosis as a percentage of interphase cells showed that on average , the dwell time of Sox2 is 54 % relative to interphase . TIRF microscope : should include a temperature - and CO 2 - controlled chamber , a stage that corrects for drift and main - tains accurate focus ( e . g . , Perfect Focus System from Nikon ) , powerful laser lines for 405 , 488 , 561 , and 639 nm wave - lengths ( e . g . , Genesis MX series 1 W ) , software - controlled Acousto - Optic Tunable Filter ( AOTF ) for precisely timed 244 James Z . J . Kwan et al . delivery of a speciﬁed amount of laser power to samples , and fast cameras designed for low light imaging ( e . g . , EMCCD ) . For detailed specs of our TIRF microscope , see [ 10 , 17 ] . 3 Methods 3 . 1 Culturing mESCs 1 . Prepare mESC Growth medium ( see Note 3 ) . 2 . Coat a 6 - well plate with 2 mL of 0 . 1 % ( v / v ) gelatin per well . Allow gelatin to settle for at least 30 min at room temperature . 3 . If starting from frozen cells , thaw mESCs with Halo - Tag fusion protein and control mESCs in a 37 (cid:3) C water bath . Transfer the contents of the vial into a 15 mL conical tube . Add 5 mL of warm fresh medium to dilute freezing medium conditions . If cells are growing in culture , proceed with passaging conditions described below . 4 . Spin at 1000 g for 4 min to pellet the cells . 5 . Aspirate the supernatant . Resuspend cells in 1 mL of fresh Growth medium . 6 . Aspirate the gelatin from the 6 - well plate . Plate the resus - pended cells onto the gelatinized plate . Add another 1 mL of Growth medium to the well , for a total volume of 2 mL medium . 7 . Grow cells for 1 – 2 days , changing Growth medium daily , until they are about 80 % conﬂuent . 3 . 2 Preparing mESCs for Single - Molecule Live - Cell Imaging 1 . Coat glass bottom 35 (cid:2) 14 mm dish with 2 mL of 0 . 1 % ( v / v ) gelatin . Allow gelatin to settle for 30 min at room temperature . 2 . From cultured mESCs , aspirate medium from the 6 - well plate . Add 2 mL of 1 (cid:2) PBS to wash cells followed by aspiration to completely remove medium . 3 . Add 1 mL of 0 . 25 % trypsin to each well to dissociate the cells . Swirl the contents to ensure the well surface is completely covered with the trypsin solution . Incubate at 37 (cid:3) C for 2 min . Observe under a microscope to ensure cells are detached from the bottom of the well . 4 . Add 2 mL of Growth medium to the detached cells to inacti - vate the trypsin . Pipette media over the well surface several times to ensure maximum recovery of cells . 5 . Transfer the cell suspension into a 15 mL conical tube . 6 . Spin at 1000 g for 4 min to pellet the cells . 7 . Aspirate the supernatant . Resuspend cells in 1 mL of Growth medium . Imaging Transcription Factor Binding on Mitotic Chromosomes 245 8 . Aspirate the gelatin from the glass bottom 35 (cid:2) 14 mm dish . Plate 200 μ L of the resuspended cells onto the gelatinized plate . Add another 1 . 8 mL of Growth medium to the well . 9 . Grow cells overnight until they are about 50 % conﬂuent for single - molecule imaging ( same for fast - or slow - tracking mode ) . 3 . 3 Labeling mESCs for Imaging 1 . Prepare mouse ES cell Imaging medium . 2 . Prepare Labeling medium . For fast - tracking experiments , use photoactivatable PA - JF646 dye ( ﬁnal concentration of 25 nM— see Note 3 ) . For slow - tracking experiments , use stan - dard JF646 dye ( ﬁnal concentration 0 . 1 nM— see Note 4 ) . 3 . Aspirate Growth medium from the glass bottom 35 (cid:2) 14 mm dish . Add 2 mL of Labeling medium to conjugate the dye to the HaloTag . Incubate for 30 min at 37 (cid:3) C . 4 . Aspirate Labeling medium . To wash cells and remove unbound ligand , add 2 mL of fresh Growth medium and incubate for 5 min at 37 (cid:3) C , followed by aspiration . Repeat for a total of three washes . On the ﬁnal wash , replace media with 1 mL of Imaging medium . 3 . 4 Single - Molecule Imaging Conditions 1 . Prepare the TIRF microscope for live - cell imaging by initializ - ing the imaging chamber and allowing for the temperature and CO 2 levels to normalize . 2 . Using epiﬂuorescence , visualize the H2B - GFP in mESCs and choose cells at the speciﬁc stages of mitosis based on chromo - some morphology . 3 . Use the crop function to isolate a region of interest ( ROI ) of about 90 (cid:2) 90 pixels ( see Note 5 ) . 4 . Turn off epiﬂuorescence and turn on the 633 nm laser at 10 % AOTF and use 100 ms exposure time . These conditions are sufﬁcient to visualize individual molecules , adjust the TIRF angle , and maximize focus . Adjusting and optimizing the TIRF angle for each cell is crucial for obtaining high - quality single - molecule images ( see Note 6 ) . 3 . 5 Fast - Tracking Acquisition and Imaging Analysis 1 . Once the TIRF angle is optimized for the ROI , program the TIRF microscope acquisition software to obtain stroboscopic excitation with the 633 nm laser ( 100 % AOTF ) at 1 ms pulses and set the exposure time to 7 ms , with 405 nm pulsing in between frames , and set the maximum number of frames to 20 , 000 – 30 , 000 ( Fig . 2 ) . The camera settings to obtain 7 ms exposure times may be manufacturer - speciﬁc . See camera man - ual for speciﬁc settings ( see Note 7 ) . 246 James Z . J . Kwan et al . 2 . After programing acquisition settings , use 100 % AOTF 633 nm laser to photobleach all spontaneously activated mole - cules . This photobleaching step will ensure that all molecules are in the dark state at the beginning of acquisition . Start acquisition . Each cell will take roughly 3 – 5 min each . 3 . For each condition ( e . g . , Mitotic vs . interphase cells ) , collect 8 – 10 cells as technical replicates for imaging . For each cell , identify cells using H2B - GFP , optimize TIRF angle and photo - bleach all activated molecules as described above . 4 . Perform the same steps for mESCs stably expressing HaloTag - only constructs to estimate fully diffusing molecules . 5 . For each imaged cell , localize and track single molecules using SLIMfast with the following parameter settings : localization error : 10 – 6 . 25 ; deﬂation loops : 0 ; Blinking ( frames ) : 1 ; maxi - mum number of competitors : 3 ; maximal expected diffusion constant ( μ m 2 / s ) : 20 ( see Note 8 ) . 6 . Determine the fraction of HaloTag - fused proteins bound using Spot - On as described in [ 20 ] with the following ﬁt parameters : TimeGap : 7 . 477 ; Gaps Allowed : 1 ; Jumps to consider : 4 ; Model ﬁt type : CDF ; Localization error : 0 . 045 . For more detailed explanation of Spot - On analysis , see [ 20 ] . 7 . For biological replicates , repeat the entire process , starting with freshly prepared cells , at least 3 (cid:2) total , acquiring 24 – 30 cells per condition . Fraction of bound molecules can be reported on a single cell basis , or as an average of biological replicates . 3 . 6 Slow - Tracking Acquisition and Imaging Analysis 1 . Once TIRF angle is optimized for the ROI , program the TIRF microscope acquisition software to acquire at 500 ms exposure time with the 633 nm laser at 10 % AOTF . The laser setting will need some optimization prior to acquiring for full experiment . To optimize , acquire 1000 frames at different laser power and use SLIMfast to localize and track molecules . Ideal laser power is the lowest amount that will result in 10 – 20 individual spots that remain distinct throughout acquisition time while also minimizing photobleaching . 2 . Depending on the expression levels of the Halo - TF protein and the labeling conditions with the dye - conjugated ligand , it may be necessary to photobleach cells to visualize individual mole - cules . This can be performed with 100 % AOTF 633 nm laser power . 3 . Once optimization of laser power as described above is com - plete , acquire 2400 – 3000 frames per cell ( 25 – 30 min per cell ) , and 8 – 10 cells per condition . Identify mitotic cells using H2B - GFP , optimize TIRF angle and photobleach all activated molecules as described above . Imaging Transcription Factor Binding on Mitotic Chromosomes 247 4 . Perform the same steps for mESCs stably expressing H2B - Halo constructs to estimate long - lived chromatin bind - ing events , which will be used as a measure of photobleaching rate . 5 . For each imaged cell , localize and track single molecules using SLIMfast with the following parameter settings : localization error : 10 (cid:4) 6 ; deﬂation loops : 1 ; Blinking ( frames ) : 1 ; maximum number of competitors : 3 ; maximal expected diffusion con - stant ( μ m 2 / s ) : 0 . 1 ( see Note 9 ) . 6 . From the output of SLIMfast , plot the survival curve ( Fig . 4 ) , which is the percent of molecules that are detectable as a function of time . Use model ﬁtting as described previously to extract empirical off - rates . 7 . Perform slow - tracking on control H2B - Halo expressing mESCs , and empirical off - rate to be used as an estimate for photobleaching rate . 8 . Subtract the empirical off - rate of the TF of interest from the estimated photobleaching rate to achieve the actual off - rate . Calculate residence time as the inverse of the actual off - rate . 9 . For biological replicates , repeat the entire process , starting with freshly prepared cells , at least 3 (cid:2) total , acquiring 24 – 30 cells per condition . Residence time can be reported on a single cell basis , or as an average of biological replicates . 4 Notes 1 . We recommend performing endogenous knockin of the Halo - Tag to a TF of interest . Whether overexpressed factors behave similarly as the endogenous proteins or not is a major concern for analyzing single - molecule imaging data . For control cell lines ( HaloTag only , and H2B - Halo expressing cells ) stable integration of constructs is also recommended . Transient trans - fections result in highly variable transfection efﬁciency and expression levels . 2 . If using other mammalian cells , follow speciﬁc growing condi - tions for that cell type . 3 . The ﬁnal concentration of PA - JF646 in the labeling medium for fast - tracking should be saturating and will depend on the expression levels of the Halo - TF fusion protein . The suggested 25 nM ﬁnal concentration will likely work for the majority of proteins , but for very highly expressed proteins , this concen - tration should be adjusted . 4 . The ﬁnal concentration of JF646 in the labeling media for slow - tracking is very sensitive to the expression levels of the 248 James Z . J . Kwan et al . Halo - TF fusion protein and should be optimized . Too high a concentration will result in an inability to detect single mole - cules even after photobleaching . 5 . For fast - tracking mode , the size of the ROI will determine the maximum frame rate possible for acquisition . Consult the manufacturer’s manual for optimal ROI size . For slow - tracking mode , the ROI size matters less . Keep in mind also that ROI size will determine ﬁnal size of data ﬁles . 6 . TIRF microscopy works best with cells growing as monolayer on the glass coverslip . However , mESCs grow in clumps . To image cells farther away from the coverslip , the TIRF angle needs to be adjusted to achieve a highly inclined laminated optical ( HiLo ) sheet . 7 . For EMCCD cameras , the mechanism for ﬁle transfer from the camera to the computer can make a difference on how fast the acquisition settings can be programmed . 8 . The above settings for SLIMfast may need to be optimized for the TIRF microscope . For instance , localization errors can be empirically measured for the speciﬁc microscope in use . Deﬂa - tion loop ( described in [ 18 , 19 ] ) can be increased to detect more particles if signal - to - noise ratio of images is an issue . Blinking parameter can also be changed to allow for “skipped” frames if signal - to - noise ratio is nonideal . The maximal expected diffusion constant is set high to allow for longer distance trajectories between frames of diffusing molecules . 9 . Many of the parameters of SLIMfast described for fast - tracking can also be used for slow - tracking , with the exception of the parameter called maximal expected diffusion constant . This parameter is set low in order to limit tracking to only bound molecules . References 1 . Rhind N , Russell P ( 2012 ) Signaling pathways that regulate cell division . Chapter 17 Light - Induced Transcription Activation for Time - Lapse Microscopy Experiments in Living Cells Jorge Trojanowski , Anne Rademacher , Fabian Erdel , and Karsten Rippe Abstract Gene expression can be monitored in living cells via the binding of ﬂuorescently tagged proteins to RNA repeats engineered into a reporter transcript . This approach makes it possible to trace temporal changes of RNA production in real time in living cells to dissect transcription regulation . For a mechanistic analysis of the underlying activation process , it is essential to induce gene expression with high accuracy . Here , we describe how this can be accomplished with an optogenetic approach termed blue light - induced chromatin recruitment ( BLInCR ) . It employs the recruitment of an activator protein to a target promoter via the interaction between the PHR and CIBN plant protein domains . This process occurs within seconds after setting the light trigger and is reversible . Protocols for continuous activation as well as pulsed activation and reactivation with imaging either by laser scanning confocal microscopy or automated wideﬁeld microscopy are provided . For the semiautomated quantiﬁcation of the resulting image series , an approach has been implemented in a set of scripts in the R programming language . Thus , the complete workﬂow of the BLInCR method is described for mechanistic studies of the transcription activation process as well as the persistence and memory of the activated state . Key words Optogenetics , Transcription dynamics , Automated microscopy , Image quantiﬁcation 1 Introduction Following reporter RNAs in living cells over time by ﬂuorescence light microscopy imaging has provided valuable information on gene regulation and RNA processing [ 1 – 5 ] . In these experiments RNA is visualized by ﬂuorescently tagged RNA binding proteins that recognize speciﬁc secondary structures engineered into the transcript like MS2 or PP7 motifs [ 6 , 7 ] . Transcription is frequently induced via the doxycycline ( dox ) - dependent binding of activator protein fusions with bacterial tetracycline repressor ( TetR ) to its high - afﬁnity operator ( tet O ) sites integrated at the promoter Yaron Shav - Tal ( ed . ) , Imaging Gene Expression : Methods and Protocols , Methods in Molecular Biology , vol . 2038 , https : / / doi . org / 10 . 1007 / 978 - 1 - 4939 - 9674 - 2 _ 17 , © Springer Science + Business Media , LLC , part of Springer Nature 2019 Electronic supplementary material : The online version of this chapter ( https : / / doi . org / 10 . 1007 / 978 - 1 - 4939 - 9674 - 2 _ 17 ) contains supplementary material , which is available to authorized users . 251 [ 8 , 9 ] . However , the start point of this reaction depends on the permeation times of dox and the association kinetics of TetR . This process occurs over a time interval of at least several minutes , which confounds the analysis of early transcription kinetics . To circum - vent this problem light - induced uncaging of an inducer molecule has been reported [ 4 ] . Yet it does not overcome the limitation that this type of chemical induction cannot be reversed quickly . While the inducer can be removed by repeated washing steps , the result - ing dissociation times are well above the minute timescale , which makes it difﬁcult to analyze the effect of a temporally limited activation stimulus . The above - described issues can be addressed by taking advantage of optogenetic approaches as for example the light - induced heterodimerization of the plant PHR and CIBN protein domains [ 10 ] . We recently developed a PHR - CIBN based optogenetic system termed blue light - induced chromatin recruit - ment ( BLInCR ) that combines rapid and reversible light - dependent recruitment of effector proteins with a real - time readout for transcription [ 11 ] ( Fig . 1a ) . The human osteosarcoma cell line U2OS 2 - 6 - 3 used in these experiments has been developed previ - ously in the Spector lab [ 9 ] . It consists of a tandem array of ~ 200 copies of a reporter gene . This reporter has lac O and tet O repeats in proximity to a minimal CMV promoter . Activator binding to the tet O sites induces transcription of a reporter RNA , which contains binding sequences for the MS2 coat protein ( MCP ) and encodes for peroxisome - targeted cyan ﬂuorescent protein ( CFP ) ( Fig . 1b ) . In this model system , transcription factor binding to the tet O sites via PHR - CIBN interactions can be controlled with high temporal precision in living cells , while RNA production is traced via tagging the MS2 reporter RNA with ﬂuorescently labeled MCP [ 11 ] . Due to the presence of multiple integration sites , transcription factor binding and dissociation as well as RNA production can be visua - lized by ﬂuorescence microscopy in single cells using conventional confocal or wideﬁeld systems ( Fig . 1c , d ) . Related approaches have been introduced by other groups and include targeting of endoge - nous genes by light - induced TALEs [ 12 ] , bacterial light - induced transcription factors [ 13 , 14 ] and targeting light - recruited activa - tors to dCas9 [ 15 ] . Here , we provide protocols for conducting experiments with the BLInCR system and for a semiautomated quantiﬁcation of the resultant image series with a set of scripts written in the R program - ming language [ 16 ] . In one type of experiments continuous blue light illumination and persistent activation is employed . The other approach described here uses pulsed activation and reactivation after an intermittent period without a light trigger during which the activator dissociates . Furthermore , image acquisition protocols for either wideﬁeld or confocal microscopy are presented . The higher spatial resolution of the confocal imaging mode allows to resolve heterogeneities inside the transcription locus ( Fig . 1c ) . ( a ) Upon blue light illumination the PHR domain switches its conformation in the PHR - GFP - VP16 construct and binds to CIBN fused to rTetR ( CIBN - rTetR ) . In the presence of dox , rTetR is bound to DNA via the tet O sequence . This leads to the fast recruitment of the VP16 transcription activator with t 1 / 2 ¼ 15 s [ 11 ] . If the blue light is switched off , dissociation occurs ( t 1 / 2 ¼ 5 min ) . ( b ) Transfecting the U2OS 2 - 6 - 3 cell line with CIBN - rTetR and PHR - GFP - VP16 allows direct Light - Induced Transcription Activation 253 addition , illumination and activation are restricted to the imaged area . For imaging larger cell numbers ( e . g . , for resolving the cell - to - cell heterogeneity of transcription activation kinetics ) , the applica - tion of wideﬁeld microscopy can be advantageous ( Fig . 1d ) . Quan - tiﬁcation of the time - lapse image series obtained in these experiments with the set of R - scripts provided yields RNA produc - tion kinetics ( Fig . 1e ) , from which information on the underlying mechanism is obtained [ 11 ] . The protocols can be easily applied to study the effects of transcriptional inhibitors or to evaluate via repeated activation cycles if the activated promoter state is “mem - orized” and how long it persists in the absence of the activator . Furthermore , we envision that our experimental approach will prove to be useful for a variety of other applications that require temporal precision and / or reversibility , for example , inducing DNA damage response by increasing the local concentration of damage factors [ 17 ] or perturbing the epigenetic landscape by recruiting modiﬁers [ 18 ] . 2 Materials 2 . 1 Cell Culture 1 . Optional : an additional reporter array marker ( e . g . , tagBFP - LacI , Addgene # 103839 ) . 2 . 3 Microscope For recording high resolution images and time series of single cells , a laser scanning confocal ﬂuorescence microscope with appropriate image acquisition software is recommended . We have used a Leica SP5 with the Leica application suite advanced ﬂuorescence ( LAS AF ) software that was equipped with the following parts : 1 . A 63 (cid:2) oil objective ( e . g . , HCX PL APO lambda blue 63 (cid:2) / 1 . 40 oil UV objective ) . 2 . A 488 nm laser to trigger recruitment and to image YFP - or GFP - tagged effector constructs . 3 . A 561 or 594 nm laser to image red ﬂuorescent constructs ( e . g . , tdTomato , mKate2 ) without triggering recruitment . 4 . Optional : a 633 nm laser to image iRFP713 - tagged effectors without triggering recruitment . 5 . Optional : a UV laser diode to record additional constructs in the blue channel ( e . g . , tagBFP - LacI as an additional array marker ) . 6 . A live cell incubation chamber that can be protected from light . 7 . A red ﬂashlight or LED to mount samples on the microscope without triggering recruitment . A removable bike tail light works well for this purpose . For automated image acquisition of a larger number of cells , a ﬂuorescence wideﬁeld microscope can be advantageous . For this purpose , we used a Zeiss AxioObserver Z1 with the ZEN 2012 pro software with modules for z - stack , time - lapse , multiposition , and heterogeneous acquisition and the following features : 1 . Live cell incubation chamber ( preferentially with black walls and a curtain for the front side ) . 2 . Motorized stage . 3 . Motorized z - drive . 4 . Hardware or software autofocus . 5 . R ( version 3 . 2 . 3 ) [ 16 ] . 2 . RStudio ( version 1 . 0 . 136 ) [ 19 ] . 3 . R packages : EBImage ( version 4 . 12 . 2 ) [ 20 ] , plyr ( version 1 . 8 . 4 ) [ 21 ] and ggplot2 ( version 3 . 1 . 0 ) [ 22 ] . 4 . R - package NSSQ “Nuclear Spot Segmentation and Quantiﬁ - cation” available in the Supplementary Materials on the book webpage . 5 . The Fiji distribution [ 23 ] of ImageJ [ 24 ] for general purpose image analysis . 3 Methods 3 . 1 Cell Culture ( Days 0 and 1 ) U2OS 2 - 6 - 3 cells [ 9 ] are maintained at standard cell culture con - ditions ( 37 (cid:3) C and 5 % CO 2 ) and split ( 1 : 6 to 1 : 10 ) every 2 – 3 days . They can be frozen in DMEM supplemented with 10 % DMSO and 40 % FCS . 1 . On day 0 , seed cells in the 8 - well slide in 400 μ L medium at a density of 2 · 10 4 per well . After adding the cell suspension to wells , let the slide stand for 5 – 10 min to obtain a homogeneous attachment of cells before transferring it to the incubator . 2 . On day 1 , replace growth medium with a reduced volume ( 300 μ L ) and prepare transfection mix according to the man - ufacturer’s guidelines in a low binding tube . For a transcription activation experiment as depicted in Fig . 1 , cotransfect cells with , for example , pPHR - GFP - VP16 ( effector ) , pCIBN - rTetR ( localizer ) , and pCMV Δ TATA - tdMCP - tdTomato ( RNA reader ) . As a negative control , use the mock effector plasmid pPHR - GFP - NLS instead of pPHR - GFP - VP16 . When using Xtreme Gene 9 ( Roche ) , use 20 μ L Opti - MEM , 200 ng plas - mid mixture ( 67 ng of each ) , and 0 . 6 μ L reagent . Place the slide in a lightproof polystyrene foam box for later transport before returning it to the incubator . The lid should be placed on the box lightly to allow air exchange . 3 . 2 Time - Lapse Microscopy for Continuous Induction ( Day 2 ) In continuous induction experiments , cells are exposed to blue light during the whole acquisition time resulting in a constant high concentration of activator at the promoter . In the following section , two imaging modes are described . If a high spatial resolu - tion is needed , confocal microscopy ( Subheading 3 . 2 . 1 ) is advanta - geous . If large cell numbers are to be acquired , automated wideﬁeld microscopy is recommended ( Subheading 3 . 2 . 2 ) . Avoid blue / Light - Induced Transcription Activation 257 white light exposure of the samples and use only red light during handling of the samples before induction . 1 . Heat the microscope incubation chamber to 37 (cid:3) C and wait for the temperature to stabilize ( 1 – 2 h ) . 2 . When using the rTetR system , add dox to the well to be imaged at a ﬁnal concentration of 5 μ g / mL before each time - lapse acquisition . Wait at least 10 min to allow for CIBN - rTetR binding to the tet O array ( see Notes 4 and 6 ) . 3 . Transfer the slide onto the microscope . Cover transparent walls of the incubation chamber with a dark cloth or aluminum foil . 3 . 2 . 1 Imaging by Confocal Microscopy By confocal microscopy the spatial distribution of RNA at the array can be assessed , which is generally not homogeneous ( Fig . 1c ) . In addition , the confocal microscopy setup can be extended to an analysis in three dimensions for data acquisition and quantiﬁcation ( e . g . , as in [ 25 ] ) to detect subtle intensity or shape changes over time . To reduce photobleaching by continuous laser exposure , cells can be exposed to blue light at regular intervals ( 2 – 3 min ) that are shorter than the reversibility half - life time of the PHR - CIBN inter - action ( 4 . 9 (cid:4) 0 . 8 min [ 11 ] ) ( see Note 7 ) . 1 . Locate cells in the red channel . 2 . Record a prerecruitment stack of the RNA reader signal ( exci - tation at 561 / 594 nm ) to ensure that no premature activation has occurred . The latter can happen due to premature light exposure during cell preparation or transport . Select a laser intensity that yields a satisfactory signal - to - noise ratio for the time course recording . Typical imaging parameters are 512 (cid:2) 512 pixel image size , zoom factor 9 corresponding to 53 nm / pixel , 400 Hz scan speed , 4 (cid:2) line average , and 5 – 8 z - slices spaced 0 . 5 μ m apart . Reducing the line average and minimizing the laser intensities can help to reduce photobleaching . 3 . Record a stack of the effector ( excitation at 488 nm ) , which will induce recruitment to the tet O - tethered localizer . For Steps 2 and 3 a single multichannel stack can be recorded by choos - ing the option to switch between stacks with the stack in the red channel being recorded ﬁrst . 4 . Record two - channel stacks of the RNA reader and the effector every 2 – 3 min for about 45 – 60 min ( see Note 8 ) . Switching the channels between lines ( as opposed to between slices or stacks ) ensures that the images of the different channels are not shifted with respect to one another . 5 . Optional : include a third channel for an additional array marker ( e . g . , tagBFP - LacI ) or a second chromatin feature probe ( e . g . , a histone acetylation reader domain ) . 258 Jorge Trojanowski et al . 3 . 2 . 2 Automated Imaging by Wideﬁeld Microscopy Wideﬁeld microscopy to monitor the onset of transcription is advantageous when assessing cell - to - cell heterogeneity of activa - tion kinetics from the analysis of tens to hundreds of cells . In addition , photobleaching is reduced due to reduced light exposure , which is particularly relevant for longer time course experiments . 1 . In a well that will not be used for data acquisition , deﬁne and test the acquisition pattern . Recommended starting values for acquisition parameters can be found in Table 2 . Locate the cells in the transmission channel with a red ﬁlter at a central position in the well with a high density of cells ( see Note 9 ) . 2 . Exposure times of the ﬂuorescence channels for each channel should be chosen between 70 and 500 ms so that there are no saturated pixels while detecting most transfected cells .
